Also, how does this differ from Easy Ubuntu or Automatix?

### Post by prizrak on 2006-11-14
> Probably the MPAA's lawyers (libdvdcss),
libdvdcss != DeCSS and was never ruled to be illegal.
> Micro$hafts lawyers (w32codecs)
The only codec MS has rights to is .wma and all of the codecs in that package are distributed at no charge.
> Adobe's lawyers(flash)
You can get it for free from Adobe, it is legal to redistribute it.
> Sun's lawyers(java)
JRE is GPL'ed...... (JDK is part GPL part CDDM)
> and Fraunhoffer's lawyers (or whatever his name was, mp3).
The rights to mp3 is owned by Thomson Corporation for one. For two RealPlayer, which is freeware in case you don't know, includes LEGAL mp3 support. Also the mp3 patent is not valid in a very large number of countries.

---

### Post by kuja on 2006-11-14
prizrak, are you sure about flash? There must be some reason that Ubuntu's repositories only include a script to download and install it as opposed to a real package. 

As per the *.wma portion of w32codecs, I'm willing to bet that if Microsoft wanted to they could blow that completely out of proportion, though yes, it's only a small part of the package.

The current java packages in Ubuntu's repositories aren't the GPL'd ones, last I checked.

I don't care about mp3 anyway, I don't use it :P

libdvdcss may not have been ruled illegal, but the use of it in certain countries is HIGHLY controversial and potentially illegal. See the [DMCA](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_Millenium_Copyright_Act) for details.

---

### Post by Snowmayne on 2006-11-14
I think the whole proprietary concept is outdated now anyway. What most people seem to forget is that these companies (Microsoft, Adobe & Real) originally made applications that used formats exclusive to them (*.xls/*.doc/*.wma, *.pdf & *.rma respectively) and while it may have been free to use viewers/players to access these files, the programs to create them were where these companies made their money. (Personally I think adobe's a bigger crook than microsoft, but that's for another thread...) 
I see nothing wrong with the whole philosophy behind ubuntu - it's why I chose to install it as my first linux os over all the other distros out there - but if someone's gonna show me a way to be able to use ubuntu (or any other linux flavor) without having to spend hours looking at discussion threads to track down how to install this app or that .tar or the other .deb file just so I can watch my DVD of X-Men 2 on my PC or play my mp3s, then I'm more than willing to give it a shot.
Sure, I could shell out a few bucks and go buy Linspire or Red Hat, or some other version of linux, but my understanding of the whole idea behind linux was that it was a free os and blah, blah, blah I'm rambling off again.
All this to say, 'good for them that decided to try making linux just that much more easier and appealing for the average shlub like me to move away from microsoft'.
(and that's my 2 cents

---

### Post by justin whitaker on 2006-11-14
I don't really see the issue here. The have gone through 2 iterations, and Feisty will cover much of what they do. 

As for the legalities...Patrick Volkerding said that, to his understanding, Frauhoffer's license covers converters like lame, not individual playback. That's why Slackware ships with MP3 playback-always has, always will. 

The same could be said for DeCSS, if I remember the rulings and the licenses correctly. Probably not. 

Flash, however, is not redistributable without prior authorization. Everywhere I look, it's always a link back to Adobe, unless they have some sort of agreement with them.

---

### Post by MethodOne on 2006-11-14
Here's a distro based on Ubuntu Edgy called Linux Mint.  It has the following features:
-GNOME
-Amarok instead of Rhythmbox
-Codecs (includes MP3, DVD, and w32codecs)
-RealPlayer
-Sun Java
-Flash 9 Beta
-Different artwork

Get it at [http://www.linuxmint.com](http://www.linuxmint.com)

I'll go download it now to see if it's any good.

Edit:  There is already discussion of this distro (or remaster) [here](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=299641)

### Post by mhancoc7 on 2006-11-14
Hi all,
I as just looking on Distrowatch and saw that the latest distro released is [Linux Mint]("http://distrowatch.com/table.php?distribution=mint"). It is based on Ubuntu and comes with win32 and other non-free codecs and plugins by default. I think it is great, but is it legal? I took a look at the site and there isn't any mention of the legalities of it. Judging from [their site]("http://linuxmint.com/"), I also do not believe that the developers have a budget for purchasing the license to distribute these codecs.

It would be awesome to know that this was legal. I seriously doubt it, but if it is I will be working on [Ubuntu Christian Edition]("http://www.christianubuntu.com") v1.6 very soon. :)

Jereme

### Post by daniel of sarnia on 2006-11-14
> **mhancoc7 said:**
> Hi all,
I as just looking on Distrowatch and saw that the latest distro released is [Linux Mint]("http://distrowatch.com/table.php?distribution=mint"). It is based on Ubuntu and comes with win32 and other non-free codecs and plugins by default. I think it is great, but is it legal? I took a look at the site and there isn't any mention of the legalities of it. Judging from [their site]("http://linuxmint.com/"), I also do not believe that the developers have a budget for purchasing the license to distribute these codecs.

It would be awesome to know that this was legal. I seriously doubt it, but if it is I will be working on [Ubuntu Christian Edition]("http://www.christianubuntu.com") v1.6 very soon. :)

Jereme

It just depends what part of the world they are from. That said MS does not sell win32 codecs for linux distros. Except for Linsprie because of a law suit they settled.

So from what I understand the only way to get win32 stuff working is through a wrapper around the widows MS codec, what most people do, that is not legal in a number of country's. Or make a win32 compatible codec in a clean room (Have one parity see the origanal source, then have that first parity wright an algorithm to show a second parity how to write a compatible codec/program from scratch. The first parity can not assist in developmint other than the initial algorithm spec.) I herd a while back Real Player was doing just that, but I do not know the status on it to this date.

In short, I think your out of luck on putting it in Ubuntu CE, for now at lest.

---

If inappropriate activity is detected, all accounts of the Customer in question will be deactivated until an investigation is complete. Prior notification to the Customer is not assured. In extreme cases, law enforcement will be contacted regarding the activity. The customer will not be credited for the time the customer's machines were suspended.

Servage reserves the right to amend its policies at any time. All Sub-Networks, resellers and managed servers of Servage must adhere to the above policies. Failure to follow any term or condition will be grounds for immediate Cancellation. You will be held responsible for the actions of your clients in the matter described on these Terms and conditions. Think people... why to make it easyer? U may use ogg for audio, etc. so open source. How many of you did change their mp3s into ogg? Is not that the way how to promote and support the open source products? To support what is open if it is as good or even better then the commercial ones?

And anyway... if people are up to learn something about linux is it really good to make it too simple for them (cause linux is sooo much more then just click-on-gui) or the windoze way (Please, if someone from ubuntu is reading - DO NEVER MAKE ANY WIZARDS MORE THAN THE INSTALLATION ONE, BECAUSE I WILL GO HARDCORE ON THEM!).

The thing about linux mint is to make it more easy to install codecs for the most part as i read on their site. Do not need such a distro - really! And i hope that in the near future more sites will support open codecs for streaming and that this will all be a discussion of the past.

### Post by ButtonMasher on 2006-11-16
> **deanlinkous said:**
> I don't see the point in this. :D

I do.  When I show Ubuntu Linux to people who are considering a switch, there's always two things that come up:

1. Why is it Orange/Brown?

2. Why do you have to install all these extra programs just to get basic things to work?

This distro seems to take care of both of those questions.  Most people don't care if it's illegal to download codecs to play music or DVDs.  They just want it to work.  And they don't want to have to install Automatix or something else to do it.  I see this distro going a long way toward spreading Linux to potential switchers...  (if they can pay their webhosting bill)  :D

### Post by Miguel on 2006-11-17
Following the trend of the thread...

Mips, this may come as something of a shock to you, but the next version of Ubuntu will ship with Java enabled by default. And no, I'm not joking. And no, this won't cause any FLOSS sofware evangelist to attack ubuntu.

Or at least I would guess so, since Java has just gone GPL.

On the issue of codecs, I understand that w32codecs might be the copyrighted ones, and, as such, illegal to use unless you have them in windows (legally). However, mp3 encoding/deconding (lame) or anything gstreamer is perfectly legal in Europe (even if France is striding towards some patents) because they do not violate copyrigth laws. They do violate software patents valid in that part of the world above Mexico (I'm not sure about Canada).

---

### Post by Miguel on 2006-11-17
Which is why ubuntu submits all patches to debian. But then, Ubuntu uploads them in a monolithic way, which makes it difficult for debian to select which patches they want. Then ubuntu complains because Debian doesn't make it clear how to contribute patches upstream. Everybody starts screaming and gals run away.

### Post by dakini on 2006-11-17
This is going to be confusing to users.  The live CD has Ubuntu branding, with no mention of Mint Linux.

The same after install - you boot into "Ubuntu", no branding and the Firefox homepage says "Welcome to Ubuntu 6.10...  The only way you can tell you're in Mint is by the blue desktop.

If you were given the lived CD by someone, you would presume that you were installing pure Ubuntu.

### Post by mhancoc7 on 2006-11-18

> This is going to be confusing to users.  The live CD has Ubuntu branding, with no mention of Mint Linux.

The same after install - you boot into "Ubuntu", no branding and the Firefox homepage says "Welcome to Ubuntu 6.10...  The only way you can tell you're in Mint is by the blue desktop.

I agree. When I started the [Ubuntu CE]("http://www.christianubuntu.com") project it was very important to me to clearly "brand" [Ubuntu CE]("http://christianubuntu.com"). 

I can tell you right now that I am pretty sure Linux Mint is a real simply customized Ubuntu using [Reconstructor]("http://reconstructor.aperantis.com"). I use [Reconstructor]("http://reconstructor.aperantis.com"), along with some other tools, to create Ubuntu CE, but I have to do some tweaking beyond [Reconstructor's]("http://reconstructor.aperantis.com") capabilities to get the "branding" just right.

Jereme

### Post by GreasedLightning on 2006-12-03
I think Linux Mint is a great idea. Many people take it for granted that others have high speed internet connections to download on. That's something you can't assume if you are trying to sell computers with a linux distro pre-installed. Ubuntu is harder to use for this because the end user would have to spend time makng their system work. 
Old computers that are being given second lives and sold cheaply with linux will most likely not be going to people with internet. 
Linux Mint is good for anyone who'll be doing lots of installations.

### Post by ubun2dragon on 2006-12-21
I am new to Linux, have tried several distros over the last couple of weeks on a Dell Dimension 2400, Celeron 2.2, 384 mb ram, with Dlink DWL-520+ wireless card.  Because I didn't have a wired connection to the router, it was very important for the distro to recognize the wireless card out of the box.  Here's a quick summary of results:

Ubuntu - Couldn't get LiveCD to boot, had to install from alternate disk.  Could not get Ubuntu to recognize wireless card.  As a new user, I don't want to have to spend hours trying to configure a wireless card, so decided to move to the next distro.

Kubuntu - LiveCD ok, install ok.  After install I could set up Dlink card and it would connect to wireless router, using default wireless manager installed with Kubuntu.  Couldn't find a way to automatically connect, had to manually open the wireless manager and connect every time I booted up.  I am trying to set up this desktop so I can remoting into it, so need to have wireless connection start automatically.  Decided to try another install.

PCLos - LiveCD ok, install ok.  After install ran a PCLos ndiswrapper wizard and card worked fine, and always connected on boot.

Linux Mint - Saw the 2.1 release came out yesterday, so even though PCLos seemed to be working fine I decided to dl Linux Mint and give it a shot.  Unlike Ubuntu, Mint LiveCD was able to boot up.  Installed without a hitch, and recognized the wireless card (and all other hardware) without any additional setup.  Thanks to Linux Mint, I finally have the distro I wanted in the first place, Ubuntu, and can now start diving into it and learning.

---

### Post by abhaysahai on 2006-12-24
I was happy with Dapper, but some devil in my ind prompted me to "upgrade" to Edgy. 
As with most of upgrades, Edgy made my system cry. It was very slow, did not recognize most of my hardware and the end result was a semi-working system. 
I thought that I'll download a Edgy iso and so a fresh install. However, I saw Mint on distrowatch and thought why not try  this. After all Mint is also Ubuntu.

I must say that Mint is "upgraded" ubuntu. Hardware recognition is better, default theme is better and the default Mint benefits like Java, mplayer, flash plugins. Mint 2.1 is so updated that it prompted me for an update just once. 
I would say that Mint is the way to go for getting a workable Desktop.

### Post by karellen on 2007-02-22
What do you think about it? for a newbie it may be great, with all the multimedia stuff and configuration tools. I didn't try it, but honestly I'm curious to see how it is...so I'm downloading the torrent right now
These are the applications added in their last release, Bianca 2.2:
- mintMenu, a replacement for the Gnome menu which is inspired by SUSE's Slab and based on USP.
- mintConfig, a Control Center application which is inspired by SUSE's configuration tool and based on UCP. 
- mintDisk, a program which automatically mounts FAT32 and NTFS (Read/Write) partitions (which are not defined in fstab) and places a shortcut on the desktop. 
- mintDesktop, a program which among other things "mounts" Windows network neighbourhoods. 
- mintWifi, which contains a guide, a collection of drivers and a diagnostic tool to make it easy for you to set up your WIFI card without having to connect to the Internet.
It seems pretty useful and user-friendly to me. Not mentioning it's based on good old ubuntu :)

---

### Post by BuffaloX on 2007-02-22

### Post by shawn_duggan on 2007-03-08
All the folks that are saying that Mint is simply Ubuntu after running Automatix are mistaken.   Others have already pointed out the additional mint* packages, and they are great, but for me, Mint just works better.

Even after installing Ubuntu several times, trying EasyUbuntu, trying Automatix, trying all kinds of stuff from these forums and wasting a lot of time, I still had problems with streaming media on my T23.  It worked, but was choppy.  It buffered like crazy.  It was not as good as from a Windows machine.  I also could never get the network browsing to work as well as it should.

With Mint 2.2, Bianca, all streaming video worked flawlessly out of the box.  It works as well as from my XP desktop.  Network browsing simply worked.

In the future KDE and XFCE versions are coming.  It's a nice distro and Clem and the folks are creating more differentiating features all the time.  The default theme isn't my cup of tea, but thats easy enough to change.

Give Mint a try.

---

### Post by beefcurry on 2007-04-16

I could simplely go on to critic it in detail, but for now I just think its trying to coppy the OSX glossy look, but not really working. In particular it does not go at all with the Tango-look alike icons and the gtk+ interface. Maybe with a better wallpaper and beryl It'll look nice, but in metacity it just dosnt quite work.

Appearance aside, you can't really judge a book by its cover. mintDisk is some feature that is something I think should've come in ubuntu along time ago, but now Automatix seems to have solved this problem on ubuntu. mint Menu is very creative. Good idea with Good functions. Works like it should and it is placed in a non instrusive way. It lacks polish and refinement, but its going in a nice direction.

Most of the good inovations has also appeared on Fiesty, such as the control panel, easy codec installation. But now since Automatix has matured alot I can pretty much improve Ubuntu alot. All the codecs and extra programs comes with a single tick box. I can get Suse Slab from Automatix, as well as mount all different partitions and make them writable.

I maybe quite harsh, but Im certainly not discouraging anyone from trying this. It is quite innovated, and hope it will always stay this innovative.

### Post by syxbit on 2007-07-04
now, before flaming me, or complaining about inclusion of codecs, hear me out.
lets make a comparison between linuxMint **light **(no added codecs) and ubuntu

isn't it embarrassing that basically one guy who already has a full time job is able to improve ubuntu quite significantly?
the menu is amazing, the config scripts are great, the extra tools for managing desktop icons, xorg, etc... are great.
theme is great too!

many will argue that a semi-competent ubuntu user could do this himself.
but my point is, ONE guy made ubuntu better. 
i honestly don't see a reason not to use mint. it's the same as ubuntu, with some added benefits. nothing is worse!
can't ubuntu learn from this? (they should hire him!)

---

### Post by kevinlyfellow on 2007-07-04
I don't see why it should be embarrassing.  Canonical spends a lot of time developing Ubuntu.  When it is given to the community, a community of even more people decided to spend time and make it even better.  In the end, the person/people distributing linux mint is completely reliant on Canonical's blood, sweat, and tears.  Canonical made their choices for Ubuntu, and the linux mint community decided to spend more time on it and refine it a bit more.  This happens a lot in open source, take a look at all the debian derivatives for example.  When sarge was waiting to be released, Mepis, Knoppix and tons of others came out and were "better".  But that isn't an embarrassment to the debian developers, its just the way it works.

---

### Post by steven8 on 2007-07-04
It's actually a tribute to the original creators.  If the original OS wasn't something special, nobody would care enough to even try and 'improve' it.

it's not comparable to the bigger changes ubuntu made over debian.
these are small, easy changes that really make a difference
surely ubuntu should have thought of some of these things.
if they don't, ubuntu is going to turn into debian; a great base for other distros, but not really used much

### Post by ThinkBuntu on 2007-07-05

shame
What could they take, really? I believe a menu that's just like mintMenu is available in the repos, they won't make codecs, etc. anymore readily available than they already are with Codec Buddy, and Mint doesn't even include mstt fonts. MintWifi is of very limited utility, and I believe that I've seen an identical ndiswrapper tool in another distro, so I believe it's already available.

The theme is subjective, and I think that overall it's good looking except for the buttons that highlight as dark blue on hover. Similarly, the Human theme is very attractive, except for the choice of color for the titlebar (just a little more orange would be nice) and the icon set. I very much like Human+Tangerice (orange tango) icons. 

mintInstall is a neat idea, but it's barely an alpha in terms of usability, not to mention that the majority of its software is already available in the repositories. Except for Google Earth, I don't see how it's a useful tool yet, but who knows?

### Post by kazuya on 2007-07-05
They are both the same to me. If anything, Mint made me appreciate Ubuntu even more. Like someone else here said, I prefer the menu settings to the Cassandra's menu options at the bottom. That is what kubuntu-desktop is for.
I use Cassandra now, due to its coming with flash, etc..
Here are is the breakdown:

I like Mint for codecs already being there. - Easily remedied in Ubuntu defaults.
I do not like the SLAB type menu as default as used in Mint - I find' the gnome menu as shown in Ubuntu more intuitive for my use.
I do not like the Mint's artwork at all. I do not hate it- So I get Ubuntu studio artwork by adding Ubuntu studio repo to my sources.list.. Very easy feat with a great howto for it.. I then move everything back to how Ubuntu is and add the extra desktop window. - Mint comes with only one desktop by default - Not my cup of coffee.

Mint is awesome, but is a sub of Ubuntu and currently they both work great for their users. Why? They have access to the Ubuntu repositories..

One thing Mint may have is the inclusion of envy in default install for install of videocard drivers.. That is also easily done in Ubuntu.. 

There is nothing to be ashamed of, it is great to have differences..

All linuxes are great to me. Do not bad mouth one over the other. Debian the original source for Ubuntu is still the grand daddy of them all. But for ease of use and per my preferences, Ubuntu more than fits the bill. So much more options for installing or getting things..

If you have a son and he is more successful than you? Are you embarrassed at his success or proud? That is what Ubuntu is to Linux Mint..

With that said, I hope your question has been satisfactorily answered?...

### Post by Bd0g on 2007-07-07

So all in all, he made a great contribution to the linux society. Just as Debian developers, Ubuntu and the rest.

### Post by weblordpepe on 2007-07-07

Hold on to your hat. Yes making new distros is a good thing and people are free to do it. I'm not forcing anything. I am pointing out that there is a big downside to having too many operating systems, all competing for attention.

I know that more choice is good, and thats fine. But somebody somewhere needs to point out that having 300 operating systems all with 3 users each competing against Windows XP does not help.

While I'm not sure what I'd do personally, I can definately see the logic in not creating Ubuntu & just sticking to Debian. But thats one option out of many, and it has up points and down points like any other choice. Its not against the linux philosophy.

Colaborating on the same project and making something big and good is a good part of the GNU ideas. Its not turning the world into Windows. If anything, Windows's strong foot in the market is to do with having a uniform, consistant desktop across the majority of computers, allowing developers a choice of platform which can hit a large market. When Ubuntu is different from Debian, and linuxmint is different from Ubuntu, then you  just make things difficult.

### Post by smoker on 2007-07-08
i've installed mint in another drive to check it out, seems quite alright so far,

imo, the more distros there are the better, everyone's idea of a perfect 'linux' is different, and as linux is getting more and more popular all the time, it is not so much a case of one distro poaching users from another, the more broader appeal a distro has, then the more popular it will likely become, and others will fall by the wayside and die. this is the natural way of things, besides, many distros are made for specialist fields and not out to gain a large following.

i see linux as an alternative to windows, not a direct competitor, even though i think in the long run it will replace windows as the desktop of choice. but i will happily use a distro with a combined total of a few hundred users if it does what i need it for, or a popular distro like ubuntu if it suits, or both, and that is my choice. and i really appreciate all those people who spend a great deal of their leisure time working away at this or that new distro, they do it for love of linux, rather than anything else.

### Post by RAV TUX on 2007-07-10

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-10
Well, what you can do is start the boot process and then press Control-Alt-F1, change the password, and then Control-Alt-F7 to log in with your new password.

---

2. Why any distro of Linux is better than the other (save for the obvious things like menu's, DE's, installers, etc...)

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-11
> **inuyokai said:**
> 
1. it's embarassing that linuxmint is better than Ubuntu if it is built off of Ubuntu and they both use the same kernel... The only thing different is the DE, right? It's actually the same DEs. Linux Mint, like Ubuntu, has Gnome, KDE, and Xfce.

It is not embarrassingly better, by the way.

The only things that are better are the default applications (Thunderbird, AmaroK, ndiswrapper, xorg-edit) included and the artwork (splash screens, wallpapers).

Some parts of Mint are actually embarrassingly worse--the default theme is ugly, and the Gnome menu turned into this ultra-cluttered menu reminiscent of the Windows Start Menu.

### Post by beast2k on 2007-07-13
Sorry to dredge up this thread again but, I had to see this distro for myself so I tried mint based on what I was reading on this thread. Overall I 'll say it still needs a little work. The start menu is better than the usual menu most distros use it's easier to find things with it. Some good tools come installed with it but they are tools that I don't think anyone would use that often unless you are a eye candy kiddy. The hardware support is not that good, my display is stuck at 1024x768, and this is a problem we ran into years ago and I thought until i used mint was more or less solved. The problems mint has are normal for beginning distros, mepis used to have that same resolution problem it took them 2 versions to finally fix it (I'm using the resolution problem as an example only because it's fresh in my mind) anyway my point I guess is Ubuntu is miles and miles ahead of mint, and theres more to  making a distro then bundling your favorite packages with another persons already established distro, most fly by night distros are done this way it seems. Like somebody already mentioned the developers of mint should have joined up with the ubuntu guys they would welcome the help.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-14
> **exploder said:**
> LinuxMint has it's own vision of what a user friendly desktop should be. 

Should the developer's of Ubuntu join up with Debian instead of making Ubuntu?
Linux Mint is essentially Ubuntu with different artwork and different default packages. It is a remaster of Ubuntu and uses direct Ubuntu repositories.

Ubuntu is its own distro built on a freeze of Debian unstable.

To be honest, I don't see what the Linux Mint devs (or dev?) would have to offer the Ubuntu devs apart from saying, "Here, have this cool-looking artwork... and can you include xorg-edit, ndiswrapper, AmaroK, Thunderbird, and Sunbird as default applications?"

### Post by ThinkBuntu on 2007-07-16
> **Extreme Coder said:**
> You're wrong.
Linux Mint is much more than pre-installed codecs. It has the SUSE-style menu, better artwork, MintInstall, which is a lot easier way to install a program, and a control center. and for some reason, it's faster.

Now here's a question for you: Did you try Linux Mint? :)
"Better artwork" is a matter of opinion. I was bugged by having to see the bloody "From freedom came elegance" everywhere, and the inane "Cassandra" branded on both the SLED menu and the login screen. I can remove it from the SUSE menu, but to get it off the login screen, you have to use an entirely different GDM theme.

I have respect for the idea behind MintInstall, but from my experience, its' a horridly buggy program. It completely locks down the system when working, and any resizing of the window is permanent, leaving you with a 380x100px progress bar, for example. Once I was installing Google Earth (which is just a binary, and needs no special installer) with the app, and it sat there for half an hour doing nothing. I opened the terminal dialogue that's embedded, and it turned out it was waiting for a simple yes/no response, and the install went fine after I agreed to the install/terms.

There's no way to pause or cancel a download, which means that your computer better not be in a hurry if you want to install a large app. Curiosity drove me to use mintInstall to add a huge game to my computer, just to see what the open source world of gaming was like (I'm not a gamer, though). I was averaging 5kb/s for a 300MB+ download with no way to quit or do anything else. I ended up having to force-restart because my computer was running too slowly to do anything in the way of significant system administration (maybe I could've tracked down the mintInstall PID, but then I run the risk of locking up Apt)

---

### Post by hboshoff on 2007-07-17
> **weblordpepe said:**
> I dont get why Edubuntu exists at all. People should just download the normal Ubuntu and load applications onto it. I don't think it warrents a new distro.

Have you ever used Edubuntu?

When one installs Edubuntu server, it has DHCP, [LTSP]("http://ltsp.org"), NFS, a separate chroot and many other things configured for thin terminals. You get a complete classroom server out of the box.

All these packages are available in *buntu, but few educators would try and set them up, or even know they existed. Installing Edubuntu is a breeze. Turning vanilla Ubuntu into a thin client terminal server is something else.

There are at least two older distros similar to Edubuntu: [K12LTSP]("http://www.k12ltsp.org") and [SkoleLinux]("http://www.skolelinux.org") (aka Debian Edu). Check them out on [Distrowatch]("http://distrowatch.com"). There is a well established niche for this type of distribution.

Using Edubuntu in standalone mode could be similar to what you propose, but that is not its main thrust.

Your lack of understanding may be the result of a very shallow analysis, no?

---

### Post by weblordpepe on 2007-07-19
Hmm....
Forgetting the idea that 'choice is good' for the moment, which everyone agrees on anyway.

There is the flip side of the coin. Every distro is competing for attention. If you have 3 or 4 voices going 'use me!' then you can just pick one, and know that there is interoperability cos most people will be using the same system.

But if you have 359 voices going 'me me me me me me me!' you only hear a noise. Its like trying to pick a puppy or kitten from the shelter. They are all cute but you want one that won't bite you in the backside.

I think that if anything - developers should concentrate on making applications and not distros. For most people, the operating system isn't something you swap and play with every time a cooler one comes out. Its the platform. Something which needs to stay and which needs a wide user base.

Now more distros is good but only if it retains 100% interoperability with the top dog.

---

### Post by RealG187 on 2007-07-19
> **buzzmandt said:**
> [http://linuxmint.com/](http://linuxmint.com/)


> it uses technologies that have been patented in some parts of the World. Most countries do not recognize the legitimacy of software patents so for most of our users this is not a problem. But if you're unlucky and you live in a country where software patents are legally enforcable, you need a version of Linux Mint which is free of patented technologies, and this is what the Light Edition is about.

> 

**Why does Linux Mint include proprietary drivers?**

It doesn't. If it did, it would be legally wrong (because it would violate the GPL) or ethically wrong (if some dirty trick was used for the user to link the code to the kernel for instance).



Dont these two quotes contradict each other?

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-19
One is talking about patented software (mainly multimedia codecs--Flash, Java, w32codecs, etc.). The other refers to including proprietary drivers integrated with the Linux kernel, which would be a violation of the GNU General Public License.

### Post by weblordpepe on 2007-07-21
Some software does not come shipped with Ubuntu because it does not follow the GPL licence. In other words the source code is not available. That means it cannot be verified & supported by Canonical.

Thats why some drivers & codecs dont come with Ubuntu. I dunno about Linux Mint though. If a codec breaks, will Linux Mint's developers help you out?

### Post by tgm4883 on 2007-07-22
> **theonlyrealperson said:**
> Seriously though, couldn't Debian users post about the same thing about Ubuntu? I'm a fan of all three distros, but c'mon...

I suppose they could try, but if you want to look at it that way then Linus could post the same thing about all linux distros.

With that being said, while Ubuntu is based off debian, it does not rely on debian (unlike mint which does rely on Ubuntu).  What I mean by that is that if debian ceased to exist today, and took all of their repositories with them, Ubuntu would function as normal (although they would have to look somewhere else for the next version).  If Ubuntu ceased to exist today and took all of their repos with them, then LinuxMint would be seriously hurt, as most of their software comes from Ubuntu repositories.  The fact that LinuxMint relies on Ubuntu in this way makes it not a seperate distro (as Ubuntu is to Debian), but a fork of a current distro.  

LinuxMint is to Ubuntu as Kubuntu is to Ubuntu.  Not as Ubuntu is to Debian.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-23
Ubuntu to Debian is not the same as Linux Mint to Ubuntu.

Linux Mint essentially *is* Ubuntu with some different default packages and artwork. It's basically a remastered live CD that can be created (and probably was created) using Reconstructor.

Ubuntu is an entirely different distribution from Debian that's built on a freeze of Debian unstable. It is not just a remastered Debian live CD.

### Post by RawMustard on 2007-07-23
I think this is a great example of how free software should really work.  It's great to see a guy that doesn't have the not made here mentality like so many distro's do.  He's taken the best of the best and put it together in an attractive package that's caught my eye, thanks for the heads up.

Ubuntu is a great distro, my fav to be exact.  I have been using it since it started, but honestly, some of the devs decisions on each release boggles my mind.  The installer is crap the theme is even more crap.  This guy has already addressed some of these things, I hope he can continue and address more of them, that's what free software is about!

All we need now is for someone to grab LinuxMint and fork gnome and we'd all be in heaven :) Or maybe KDE 4 and LinuxMint will do just that :)

I just wanted to add:

This bit here sounds really great.  Wasn't ubuntu going to implement that click'n'Shaft rubbish from that other mob?  Perhaps this would be a better solution seeing as this guy hasn't made a pact with the devil.

CL: MintInstall is my favourite innovation. You browse a software portal containing .mint files, you choose your software and one click later it's there in your menu. Before mintInstall you had to browse the Web to find that software, eventually edit your sources.list with a new repository, add a security key for that repository to work, update your cache and eventually find the name of the package(s) you wanted to install. This was a repetitive task and an obstacle for novice users to install third party software on their systems. We've automated this with mintInstall. The .mint file contains all the instructions for the installation of the software (names of the packages, URL for the repositories, their keys, instructions to run before and after the installation) so the user doesn't need to worry about things that do not matter to him. The portal also acts as a central place where users can search for software packages and browse them by category. Finally, mintInstall doesn't use your own sources.list and it doesn't alter it so your system is safe and your sources remain unchanged. It replaces the need for both Gnome-App-Install and the upcoming Click'N'Run.

### Post by ThinkBuntu on 2007-07-23
> **RealG187 said:**

Truly, Mint **is** Ubuntu. Mint uses Ubuntu packages, repositories, etc. But Mint does have its own forum and also has a few pieces of original software, which consist of a very young installer app, a few system configuration utilities, and a Applications menu called mintMenu.

Only in certain countries, where software law is restrictive, is including all this software illegal. The US is one such country.

### Post by athertek on 2007-07-26
When i first started reading this and people started saying linux should be consolidated into one big distro i agreed. I've thought about it though and realized that open source and linux is like its own little ecosystem. 

Linux distributions come and go, the only really popular one that's been around basically forever is RedHat, maybe Debian too, which I think will last. Ubuntu I think will last too, but that's the thing about linux is that it evolves. 

I think yes, there are too many distros and if all the work had been combined into one distro, we would have an incredible product which would be very beneficial to Linux's popularity.

However we can't force people to work on Ubuntu, I think that as Linux matures it will narrow down to a few big distros. Open source software has its own kind of natural selection thing (which I think is great by the way), products come and go but in the end I think a few will come out on top, so there's nothing to really worry about. Yes there are tons of small distros, but they always die off and the really innovative features and ideas are merged into the bigger distros.

The way I see it the ideal outcome is that eventually Linux will narrow down to a few distros. A server distro, office/workstation distro, and home/multimedia distro (or something like that). Or maybe one distro with those flavors.

### Post by fistfullofroses on 2007-07-26
It all depends upon what you mean by "better." I say screw them all. I make my own flavor of Ubuntu everytime I install. I start with the Xubuntu AMD64 Alternate disc. I then choose command line install from the boot menu, and build my own "flavor" package by package. I am still using "Ubuntu Linux" buy my own version of it. All a distribution is, is a set of artwork and default packages. Sometimes (as is the case with Ubuntu) a company or group of programmers will make custom programs to go with their distro. Red Hat's RPMs are a technology from Red Hat. Debian's DEBs are another. Ubuntu's upstart and their update programs are two more. Yum from Terrasoft is yet another example. These are all small differences though. A person can start with one distro and turn it into another if so inclined. Your post was one of the utmost stupidity.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-30
> **Minki said:**

Friendlier config for Nautilus desktop
Graphical ndiswrapper config and ndiswrapper
A different (in my opinion, worse) Gnome menu
Different artwork and theme
Different default programs (Thunderbird and Sunbird instead of Evolution, AmaroK instead of Rhythmbox)

The current version of Ubuntu (Feisty) and the new version of Ubuntu (Gutsy) are unaffected as they have already gotten everything they need from Debian.  All of their packages are already compiled for Ubuntu (Most of LinuxMint's packages are compiled for Ubuntu).

Is this really so hard to comprehend?  I can draw a picture if I need to.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-08-01
And my point is *no*, Ubuntu won't go further up the chain and develop for themselves. From [the Ubuntu Wiki](https://wiki.ubuntu.com/ContributingToDebian): > Ubuntu benefits from a strong Debian, and Debian benefits from a strong Ubuntu. - MarkShuttleworth

Every Debian developer is also an Ubuntu developer, because one way to contribute to Ubuntu is to contribute to Debian. - MarkShuttleworth

**We incorporate Debian changes regularly, because that introduces the latest work, the latest upstream code, and the newest packaging efforts from a huge and competent open source community. Without Debian, Ubuntu would not be possible.** - MarkShuttleworth 

---

### Post by tgm4883 on 2007-08-01
> **aysiu said:**
> And my point is *no*, Ubuntu won't go further up the chain and develop for themselves. From [the Ubuntu Wiki](https://wiki.ubuntu.com/ContributingToDebian):

While thats nice and all, if Debian ceased to exist I have a hard time seeing Ubuntu go "Well thats it everybody.  We had a good couple years.  Thanks for all the hard work.  Have a great life."

IMO, I would think that the above quote is more a friendly gesture toward the Debian Developers.  They do a lot of great work.  But if Debian ceased to exist today, I think the Ubuntu devs would look for another option.  I don't see them throwing away years of hard work.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-08-01
And I have a hard time believing that Debian would suddenly cease to exist. It's more likely that Ubuntu would cease to exist. Ubuntu is primarily funded by  Mark Shuttleworth and the Ubuntu Foundation (which is really just Mark Shuttleworth, anyway), and it's been around for less than three years. Debian has been around for almost fourteen years and is maintained by a worldwide network of volunteers, not primarily funded by any one person.

Your "what if" scenario makes no sense, since Debian isn't going to up and disappear with Ubuntu still remaining.

Besides, you missed that last part of the Wiki quotations. Ubuntu incorporates Debian changes regularly. That is not merely a nod to Debian developers. That is a statement of logistical fact.

Sure, in some alternate dimension if Debian ceased to exist, Ubuntu wouldn't pack up its bags, but it would have to seriously change the way it currently operates. If Debian ceased to exist, Ubuntu would probably try to recruit all the Debian developers for Ubuntu or for something like Debian, or Ubuntu would base itself off Gentoo or Fedora.

You should do the same or not, if you don't want to, but don't post ignorant comments.

Billionaires, not that I know of, but there is at least 1 millionaire

### Post by aysiu on 2007-08-03
> **floke said:**
> 
People need to stop the whole 'Mint isn't better because its really Ubuntu' thing, because no-one is claiming any differently. Actually, I don't recall *anyone* saying anything remotely like "Mint isn't better, because it's really Ubuntu."

The reason people are saying that Mint is just Ubuntu with tweaks is to counter the notion that Mint is such a good distribution *on its own* that Ubuntu developers should be embarrassed. Well, there's nothing to be embarrassed about, because Ubuntu developers did most of the work that Mint benefits from.

It's not to say Mint *isn't* better *because* it's really Ubuntu. It's to say that Mint isn't an embarrassment to Ubuntu developers, since they're the ones who are working the hardest to create a base for Mint.

Whether Mint is actually better or not is a matter of opinion and has nothing to do with the fact that it's modified Ubuntu. Some people clearly like Mint better. I happen to like vanilla Ubuntu better.

### Post by WKnew on 2007-08-13
I am sometimes amazed at how fast a discussion can garner that not invented here tone and disintegrate into FUD. 
Please think about the roots of this fine open source model we all enjoy. It isn't about what is right for me so it should be right for you. That kind of thinking is diametrically opposed to the foundation of Linux. 

A quote from Linus himeself:
"And the whole open source thing is not an anti-MS movement either. ... Open source is a model for how to do things, and I happen to believe that it's just a much better way to do things and that open source will take over not because of any battle, but simply because better ways of doing things eventually just replace the inferior things."

Read the whole interview here. 

[http://www.computerworld.com.au/index.php/id;377002614;pp;1;fp;2;fpid;4](http://www.computerworld.com.au/index.php/id;377002614;pp;1;fp;2;fpid;4)

Let's not implode from within because we like things slightly different.

### Post by mdsmedia on 2007-08-18
Simply put, Mint is a derivative of Ubuntu. If Ubuntu want to use the revisions that have taken place in Mint, they can.

Saying that one distro, based on another, is an embarrassment to the other, is simply ridiculous.

The "improvements" to Ubuntu that have been included in Mint CAN be used in Ubuntu, IF Ubuntu wants to implement them.

That's Open Source.

### Post by Visti on 2007-09-13
I installed a clean Linux Mint the other day (Cassandra) - I've had it lying around for a bit, so it's not the newest iteration. It's been getting a lot of good response, so I thought I'd try it out. And I was surprised. Looking at it's feature list, it just looks like a slightly embellished Ubuntu, but the result is greater than the sum of its parts.

 It just feels a lot better and to me, the menu system, the way the controls are set up and, heck, even the small things like the tiny MintDesktop menu just feels right and intuitive. In fact, I just installed Gutsy in the same go and was trying it out afterwards and my first thought was how hard it would be to get a menu system set up that's similar to the one Mint uses.

Some of the ideas seem weird (MintInstall?), but I really encourage Mint. It has really impressed me.

### Post by angryfirelord on 2007-10-23
> Thing is Ubuntu is better that debian...
That strongly depends on what you use your distro for. If someone needs a debian system out-of-the-box, then that's what Ubuntu does well. However, Debian tends to give a lot more flexibility in what you want to do with it and there are still some kinks that Ubuntu needs to work out, such as providing security updates for the universe and multiverse packages (Debian guarantees security updates for all of its pacakges, not just in the "main" section).

### Post by Scotty Bones on 2007-10-26
> **bogolisk said:**
>  Feisty finally fix a kernel bug long present in both Dapper and Edgy. My mainboard now can boot with usb-legacy enabled in the BIOS. I don't think  the LinuxMint dev has any clue about this fix.

Nor should he. Thats not for him to fix.  Mint isn't about getting under the hood and repairing the engine, thats ubuntu's job or whomever's job it is to work on the kernel.  Mint is more about the superficial (like wood grain dashboards and extra cup holders).  The additions to Mint are mostly from community generated ideas, like "hay, wouldn't it be great if we had so and so to make something A easier to do".  Thats what Mint is about - community driven innovation to help make the **Ubuntu** experience just a little bit better.  

Side Note:
Just because you in your infinite experience can easily make the changes yourself, doesn't necessarily mean a noobie can.

---

I love it..... :)

### Post by foggytown on 2007-12-09
This is a great thread, a neat historical perspective...but I confess that I didn't read it all.

A recent hardware gotcha recently forced a back-grade from Mint Daryna to Ubuntu Gutsy, so it was interesting to read the perspectives posted above.  I've been running the most recent versions of Mint for most of the year and have been most satisfied--satisfied enough to end my distro hopping days.

Mint is more than just Ubuntu+theme+codecs as many believe that it is.  It is the whole package from application choices to configuration defaults and beyond .  For me, Mint requires little after install configuration--things just work.  Of course it is not perfect for me, I must still uninstall/install numerous packages to make it work for me.  It just takes less work (than any other OS I have ever used).

But saying this is not to put down Ubuntu.  Without Ubuntu, Mint is nothing.  The charm of Mint/Ubuntu (IMO) is that for any problem you simply type into google "problem description gutsy" and you get the answer/solution or directions to it.

If Mint were not so closely tied to Ubuntu, the above would not be the case--so it is good that it not depart too far from its base.  The Ubuntu community are a great resource.  I've been coming here for solutions since I first started with Mint.

From reading above, I see that MintMenu is not well liked, I suggest giving the newest version a try...
In software sources add:
[http://www.linuxmint.com/repository/](http://www.linuxmint.com/repository/) daryna main upstream import

Then search for and install MintMenu using Synaptic.  Right click on a panel and choose "add to panel" and there will be a new entry under Misc.  (Don't forget to go back and disable the mint repo in software sources)

So the point of this post is that I really like Mint and think Clem is doing a phenomenal job.  But without Ubuntu, Mint wouldn't be.  So I give props to both teams....

---

### Post by Gustave on 2007-12-17
I posted about a problem I have been having with Gutsy, and someone mentioned Mint.  This was yesterday. 

After fresh installing Gutsy today, and getting everything set up, I was STILL having problems with playing some streaming video online, with one video player not working, with VNC video jumping around, and other annoyances. ### Post by adam.skinner on 2007-12-21
> **jseiser said:**
> is it even legal to package these codecs with a release?

These codecs are freely available from their sources.  They're just not "open source", and hence have a stigma.  The fact of the matter is that the majority of Linux users use closed source material on their computers, and consider the acquisition of this material part of a standard install prep.

Mint rolls this prep into the install, and makes sure it "just works" right off the bat.  This pleases me.

---

### Post by bromix on 2007-12-21
I'm an Ubuntu user.  My father has switched to Mint.  He loves it, and I love Ubuntu.  Ubuntu "just works" for me, so never saw a need to switch to anything different.  People say Mint looks pretty, I can make Ubuntu look however I want it to.  One poster said the Mint Menu drew him in.  There are a handfuil of different options for a menu in UIbuntu, a few of which peform and look the same.   As far as codecs, etc...I can understand Canonical's standpoint on not "enabling" them by default.  That's why I have never had a problem opening Add/Remove and simply ticking a few boxes, then clicking "Apply" to enable them.   I think when we explore different distros, our decision and opinion should be what works best for ourself and our particular tastes.  As well as our individual hardware configurations.  These things themselves dictate a very wide spectrum of needs in an operating system.   And that, my friends, is the benefit of having many different distros to choose from.  Another distro should be considered another joining the force, not another foe to fight.

### Post by DJiNN on 2007-12-22
> **Angelbeast said:**
> But what is the diff between the gnome release and xfce?

As far as i know (& i have run both) it's just basically that.... The **XFCE** version has **XFCE** (Plus more **GTK** stuff than Gnome) and the **GNOME** version has **GNOME** (Obviously) and is far more **Gnome** biased.  The **XFCE** version is "Lighter" but certainly no less functional, and has many "Bells & Whistles" that you'd think you'd only get with a "Heavier" distro. 

If you're running a **64bit CPU** i'd go with the **Gnome** version (Which is what i'm using right now) as it's superb. If you have a decent Graphics card you'll also get Compiz working really well, and if you want to make it really fast, try using **OpenBox** with it (As the Window Manager).... it's amazingly light for a Gnome distro.

Having been using **Xubuntu** for the last 2 years, i finally gave up on having to get XFCE to do Net Shares everytime i re-installed. So i installed Mint 4 & It's terrific! It just does everything i want & really well. 

try it..... i think you'll like it. :)

> aand by the way for the people who are saying it's bad to have something work outof the box because no one will learn...I have to disagree...I want it to work but i also like to poke around under the hood and learn how it works :-)

Absolutely!!  It's much easier if something works & **THEN** you start playing, rather than **HAVING** to muck around with it just to get it working! :)

### Post by exploder on 2007-12-22
LinuxMint is nice because you can have your system fully up and running in a very short amount of time. I installed Mint, updated it, tweaked it, loaded Brasero, Xnest, Adblok Plus, setup mail and printer, installed ccsm, and emerald in 40 minutes! 

Another nice quality is that I do not have to spend time making Mint look good, it looks good by default! I like the fact that I can have a root account if I need one too. Mondo will not make a usable backup using sudo, you need to be root. I like having a choice with a root account and a couple of mouse clicks will enable or disable the root account.

The Mint menu is awesome! I do not need any desktop shortcuts at all. The favourites section keeps everything I use on a daily basis handy and the menu is pleasing to the eye.I like a clean desktop and the Mint menu keeps things neat.

The new User Guide is a great source of information to user's that are new to Linux. The guide is well organized and is easy enough for anyone to understand.

In my opinion, yes Mint really is that good!

choice for them, though of course there is huge demand from real users
for that. And we will stay firm in that regard. Ubuntu does not, and
will never, include proprietary applications."

Call it propaganda if you want, but I respect it and to me it's what Linux is all about in the first place.

### Post by finferflu on 2007-12-23
I have two things to say. 
The first is about Free Software. What Stallman says is that we shouldn't use proprietary software in order to discourage its propagation. So what distros like Mint are doing is spreading more and more proprietary software, hindering acceptance of open standards. For example, if the vast majority of people keep using MP3, Ogg will never spread so much to take over it. 
On the other side I have to say that we don't have really appetible open source alternatives, as far as I can see. And I don't mean, for example, Gnash vs. Flash, I mean something that does what Flash does, but better. So while I respect the Free Software philosophy I am disappointed with the current results of open source alternatives, but I hope in the future I will be able to completely switch to Free Software. 

Secondly, I have to say that for some people the real fun is in customising the distro to the very detail. I guess this does not really apply to most of the Ubuntu users, but I enjoyed tweaking and trying to make things work, and eventually switched to Arch, which gives me more space for customisation. 

I might try Mint out of curiosity anyway, I love to see how pre-packaged distros work out of the box.

---

i have tried mint live cd, i might install it on another computer and give it a try one day but i really cant wait till hardy to come out :D

### Post by new2*buntu on 2007-12-24
> **MangasColoradas said:**
> 
I now have lost the recovery-mode option for Ubuntu in Grub, I would like that back.

I think you could probably go to your Ubuntu installation partition, then navigate to /boot/grub/menu.lst. Open it with Gedit (It is OK if you have no root privileges for now) and scroll down until you find the Ubuntu 7.10 kernel (recovery mode) section (with the title and root and parameters). Now leave that open and go into the terminal and type ```
sudo gedit /boot/grub/menu.lst
``` Go to the Ubuntu section and skip one line, then copy the Recovery mode section from your Ubuntu partition to the Ubuntu section in your Mint partition. Save and reboot. You should now see the Ubuntu recovery mode.

---

### Post by P235 on 2007-12-25
I do agree that codecs are easy to install, but, as a user, I still find the matter of installing the correct restricted packages to be a bit of a mystery.  For instance, "[Enabling Multimedia in Feisty (HOW-TO)]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=413624")" from the beginner's forum, instructs users to install a number of gstreamer packages.  However, there is another link in [Ubuntu Documentation]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/RestrictedFormats") that says users can install the ubuntu-restricted-extras package to enable restricted multimedia formats.  These threads are merely a sample of the total number of different instructions available on Ubuntu forums and while I applaud the Ubuntu community for having guides available for installing multimedia features, the many different sets of instructions make me worry more than anything else.  As a character who tends to prefer things spelled out clearly, what are the differences between these packages?  Can someone from 7.04 install the ubuntu-restricted-extras package and be done with it all?  What set of instructions truly, without fail, enable all of the multimedia formats that I have learned to expect from a Windows machine?

Ultimately I followed a combination of guides, installed a mix of recommended packages, and decided enough things work for me to stop worrying about the matter.  Yet, happy ending aside, the idea of installing a cocktail of codec, flash, and dvd playback packages I only vaguely understand to be required for a desired result was hardly pleasant.  A lot of the instructions I found on these forums simply offered instruction and the understanding of such instruction were somewhat axiomatic.  How do the people who write these guides come to the conclusion that the recommended packages were the correct ones to install?  What exactly does ubuntu-restricted-extras install?  What is it missing that I may wish to be aware of?  Perhaps my questions are merely the result of my single tracked mind, but sometimes the lack of online, fleshed out introductory guides to Linux concepts and tutorials bother me.  Of course, if I were still using Windows, I would have installed something like K-Lite, CCCP, or some other mysterious codec package with the same leap of faith as in Ubuntu with gstreamer and restricted-extra packages!

Thus I find that regardless of the OS, enabling multimedia features entail the same set of problems.  Mint simply side steps the issue entirely by making everything "work out of the box" with the minimum effort required of the user, yet beginners are still none the wiser.  Instead of the Mint approach, I suggest that the Linux community should review the way it offers its online resources and revise them to communicate fully and clearly to a broader audience (namely a Windows-to-Linux or Mac-to-Linux or whatever have you audience).  In my case, just looking at the two links above, it would have been less worrisome if a multimedia guide said something like, doing X, Y, and Z will install A, B, and C, but not D.  

I find it strange how someone suggested there is some stigma attached to closed multimedia formats.  The link to Ubuntu documentation clearly explains why a user must install the restricted extras himself.  The use of such restricted packages are not discouraged.

---

### Post by MangasColoradas on 2007-12-25
Mint is a seperate distro built on Ubuntu so...you will have to install it, not upgrade to it.

You will get the Ubuntu updates when using Mint.

Anything you are doing with Ubuntu you can do with Mint, Mint is just Ubuntu with a few tweaks and its own upgrade system, with updates from the Mint servers and Ubuntu repos.

**Mint PC - AMD64 3200/512MB RAM/Nvidia 6200 128MB/MSI Neo2 Plat**

1) problem recognizing the display/settings but _every_ distro to date has had problems with the KVM switched 19" LCD.  All of them want to set it to 85Hz, which the monitor can't handle.  I changed a few settings and it was happy.  I even installed the Nvidia driver for 3D with a few clicks, not bad.  For some reason this reset the display from 1280x1024 to 1024x768, poop no GUI option back to the higher resolution

2) I had to copy a file (menu.lst?) from grub to boot because I set up a seperate boot partition which their system didn't like.

3) Mint 32bit is a little slower, but it was also using a little disk swap space on the old PC with 512MB, which the Ubuntu PC does not because of 2GB RAM.  Synaptic and updating is slower than in Ubuntu 64 too.

4) I am not sure I like the Mint menus as is, but I liked the clean Minty desktop theme

5) Mint ran DSLreports java and flash speed tests out of the box, Ubuntu 64 did not without tweaks, understandable.

6) Amarok worked very slick with Shoutcast out of the box in Mint, I liked it.  I didn't try it with Streamtuner/ripper yet however.  Sound on this old rig usually was not too good but was actually better than the newer PC running Gutsy, strange.

7) I can't believe they didn't install GnomeBaker.  Easy to fix but come on people, gotta have it to test burn the Fedora disk.

Mint 4 is a very nice distro and one I'd really recommend for a newb.  I am considering for our daily use PC actualy.

I tried a quick Fedora 8 64bit KDE LIVE test on the Mint box tonight and it did not go well.  It set the right resolution, but not the refresh (input out of range) and there was no way to reset it that I could find.  It was running slow like something was thrashing in the background then it said that the networking was configured.  It was still slow then popped up some funky message about the sound system overloaded the CPU or something similar.   Taps please.

The Bad:

### Post by whiteraven on 2007-12-28
Sorry if this has been asked before, but can't seem to find an answer.

I'm going to try Linux Mint and would like to know before I download:
What does the term Community Edition mean with respect to the various Linux Mint releases?? (as opposed to Main and Light?)

Thanks in advance

---

### Post by elithrar on 2007-12-28
The "Community" releases are not the 'mainstream' releases and use alternative window managers (XCFE & KDE) instead of the default Gnome environment in the main build. I would recommend downloading the Linux Mint "Main" ISO as this is the most 'tested' variant.

### Post by elithrar on 2007-12-29
There's not a whole lot of difference if your Ubuntu install is solid, but if you're having a few niggles with Ubuntu, Mint might be a good alternative. It really comes down to personal preference though. I'm considering convincing a relative into buying a new notebook and putting Linux Mint on it for them - the learning curve would be no different from XP > Mint than it would be for XP > Vista - except they'd have a much faster and secure OS.

---

### Post by kamaboko on 2007-12-29
I was having problems with Ubuntu.  Nothing major, but just enough little things to piss me off.  So, I gave Mint a shot.  I really like it.  It's fast and stable.  In the mean time, I have installed Virtualbox and played with a couple of other flavors: Debian Etch, Suse 10.3, and Fedora.  The thing I like about Mint is that all the stuff I need is installed and ready to go.  For instance, today I was messing with drivers for a Nvidia 8400m GS video card to work with Debian.  Nothing was working.  I tried three "solid" guides and all of them failed.  After a few hours I was thinking, "why the hell am I dealing with this when Mint had it down from the get go?"  

I don't think an OS should be a big F'ing erector set.  I just expect some things to work out of the box; wireless and video being two of them.  God, Vista does it.   

I'll give Hardy a shot when it comes out, but in the mean time it'll be Mint.

### Post by GSF1200S on 2007-12-31
Mint may be all that, but whats the big deal?

Its really a pretty good idea to learn how everything falls into place, including flash/java/codecs. I mean if the average user has no idea, he/she wont know the fundamentals of their own system. While im not saying we should go back to the first release of Slackware, I say its definitely a good thing that we at least realize the proprietary elements of our OS and its general function. It leaves us more informed, which allows us to make better decisions and appreciate the position of our chosen distro/OS. Besides, how hard is it to install this stuff? Never took me more than a few minutes, and im on 64bit! Rather than bundling everything with the install, making it a two click process accomplishes ease and being informed.

And who really thinks they can say that Mint is what Ubuntu should have been? What about what Mint doesnt have: an alternate CD for instance. I for one dont much care for what everyone thinks ill need. I like to build my gnome or kde or fluxbox or icewm or openbox from a command line install with apt- at least then I choose EVERYTHING that goes into my system.

Im glad that you all like Mint and im sure its great, but there are other philosophy's that exist beyond your own and they are just as important.

---

### Post by snakeeyes on 2007-12-31
Ok this my thinking, Debian is the reason Ubuntu exists right? Now we all exist because of our parents, but r we our parents? No, we r completely different even though we carry the same gentic code, we may look and behave similar to them, but we will be unique.

Same way Ubuntu and Mint r both seperate and unique. Debian developers knew when they made a Linux distro that people may modify it and make their own distributions from it.

Lets say if Debian may not have existed who knows Ubuntu would have been the first distro to create a deb package.

I like Debian as distro, but we r seperate and they r seperate.

### Post by SomeGuyDude on 2008-01-01
> **snakeeyes said:**
> Ok this my thinking, Debian is the reason Ubuntu exists right? Now we all exist because of our parents, but r we our parents? No, we r completely different even though we carry the same gentic code, we may look and behave similar to them, but we will be unique.

Same way Ubuntu and Mint r both seperate and unique. Debian developers knew when they made a Linux distro that people may modify it and make their own distributions from it.

Now we all love and respect our parents right because they r the reason we exist, same way users of Debian based systems users like Ubuntu and Mint users respect and may like Debian, but we don't owe them anything. Lets say if Debian may not have existed who knows Ubuntu would have been the first distro to create a deb package.

I like Debian as distro, but we r seperate and they r seperate.

Whoa whoa, though. We're not talking something like parents here, we're talking direct building upon foundation. If you like a quality about someone, it's not a given that that quality exists because it existed in the parents. The parents were merely the way the child spawned.

In this case, a whole lot of what is enjoyed about Ubuntu is because that's something done in Debian. A lot of what is enjoyed in Mint is because it's a feature of Ubuntu. They are their own entities and each has features that make them unique, but to make the claim that they don't owe anything to their bases is just odd.

### Post by RS3York on 2008-01-01
> **snakeeyes said:**
> Yeah Mint is based on Ubuntu.

No saying Mint is better than Ubuntu is not like saying Ubuntu is better than Linux because Ubuntu is Linux whereas Mint is not Ubuntu. Mint is another Linux distribution no matter whats it based.

Mint doesn't owe anything to Ubuntu nor does Ubuntu owe anything to Debian either cause if u have not forgotten Ubuntu is based on Debian. According to ur description Ubuntu should be grateful Debian existed right?

Also Sabayon is based on Gentoo Linux and I like it a lot better than Gentoo. Ubuntu isn't the only high profile distribution in the Linux world, we have many much more powerful distros like Redhat, Sabayon, Gentoo, and openSUSE and don't forget PCLinuxOS.

PCLinuxOS also has many codecs by default, Sabayon has proprietary drivers present in it as well as codecs. Are u calling them small time unknown Linux distros?

I agree that Windows doesn't have flash installed by default either but we r not Windows users, we r Linux users so lets not compare ourselves with an inferior product to Linux.

Hmm, I disagree with this too.

While I agree that it's true there are other "high-profile" distros I don't think anyone would dispute the proposal that if we had to single out 1 distro as *the most* high-profile... right now that distro would be Ubuntu.  Furthermore, a year ago, the same question would have yielded the same answer.

The only other ways I can imagine would be a business angle at which point the case for Red Hat & Novell offerings become obvious.  Or the server market where Debian is also strong.

The supposed use of PCLOS is overrated & overstated, the only evidence people seem to supply its Distrowatch rank.  I'm not taking a shot at the quality of the distro (it's pretty nice) but its actual popularity beyond people being curious and clicking the #1 spot at Distrowatch is certainly high...if we consider its place among ALL distros.  But from what is available it appears PCLOS isn't even a Top 5 distro in use for personal users[1].  And since personal desktop use is (apparently) so lopsided towards Debian+Ubuntu once you add in openSUSE the field starts to get into statistical 'deadheats' (to be fair to your point Gentoo did come #4, followed by Fedora).

If you want to talk about people with their minds in the linux-sphere then sure, Sabayon & PCLOS certainly are well known.  But beyond that sphere, I think it's easy to label both of them "small time unknown Linux distros".

Put succinctly...Lumping a bunch of distros into a class of "high-profile distros" does not mean they are equally high-profile.

In addition, the comparison of Debian|Ubuntu and Ubuntu|Mint is a stretch.  The level of work done by *buntu developers on top of the Debian base compared to the level of work performed by Mint on top of Ubuntu isn't close.  Let's not get fooled by changes in art.  Besides, the both of them (Ubuntu & Mint) do owe some thanks to their bases - Especially Mint, considering that its users rely directly upon the Ubuntu repos for software and most updates.

Again, I'm not trying to say any of those distros are low quality, I've used recent versions of all of them (except pure Gentoo) and I can see why people would like each of them.

The reason why Ubuntu doesn't include codecs is philosphical and just plain wise.  Why unnecessarily expose Canonical to potential litigation because a section of users can't be bothered to install ubuntu-restricted-extras & libdvdcss?  It should come as no surprise that the distros from groups that plans on being deployed in businesses (esp. in the USA) do not include propriety codecs in their free editions.  Since Ubuntu doesn't have a "pay for a licence edition" it doesn't and won't ever include the propriety codecs OTB.

The comment about Flash & Windows is a great point.  Windows doesn't have Flash by default so this argument people push about how distros should ship codecs because "users can't be trusted to install add-ons without running back to Windows" doesn't make sense.  There are also a number of codecs that aren't in Windows.  Hence the popularity of codec packs for Windows.

[1][ Desktop Linux Survey 2007 ](http://www.desktoplinux.com/cgi-bin/survey/survey.cgi?view=archive&id=0813200712407)

---

### Post by SomeGuyDude on 2008-01-01

You can like Mint better if you want, that's cool. I have it dual-booted, but if you're denying that Mint relies on Ubuntu in the way my car relies on its wheels then you're just being obstinate.

### Post by RS3York on 2008-01-01
