> Ok, Ubuntu really isn't the most high profile distro. I had heard about Suse 4 years before I heard about Ubuntu. The only reason I heard about Ubuntu was from youtube. I use Kubuntu 7.10 as my main desktop. I think we should stop with this pride about being the best. Remember those with pride always fall. 

Perhaps I did a poor job in conveying tone in my response.  

It's not about any "pride" I have in Ubuntu, openSUSE is my primary distro!  Still, I don't try to hide or confuse the point that right now Ubuntu is the #1 linux distro for personal desktop linux users.  To my knowledge, there's no data suggesting otherwise beyond the HPD counter at Distrowatch.  And the HPD counter says that PCLOS is the most popular which is patently false if we are counting actual use and not just what name gets clicked the most.  If you know otherwise, please provide a link to the data.  Until then, I don't see how there is a disagreement to be had concerning this.

4 years ago there was no Ubuntu at all (at least for mass consumption) so what's the relevance?  At any particular point in time something will be #1 (a tie is possible obviously but that's not the case here).  Of course what is most popular changes over time, no one disputes that, but if we're talking in January 2008 about which distro has the highest profile then that's Ubuntu.  I don't see what's so controversial or full of pride that fact is.  If in March 2009, Ulteo Linux is the most popular, that's fine.  But when we look back at January 2008 we'll be able to say that *at that time* Ubuntu was most popular.

> 
Another thing PCLinuxOS is a great distro, even though I don't use it many people have had a great time with it.


I said more than once that I was not commenting on PCLOS (or any distro's for that mattter) quality/appeal as a distro.  I was making comments about how popular it is since you made a claim concerning which distros qualify as "high-profile".  I never once stated PCLOS was bad or otherwise not good.

> 
I learnt to use a computer using Windows but do I owe Microsoft anything, the way u say it majority of Linux users have windows backgrounds so we should go bow down to Microsoft right? After all they r the ones we learnt to use a pc the first time. I don't think like this, we don't owe Microsoft anything if Microsoft hadn't made Windows I would have used another OS, simple as that.


Nowhere did I say anybody needs to "bow down"  to anybody else.  I also haven't said anyone needs to worship, polish the shoes of, or kiss the rear-end of anybody else so hopefully you won't suggest any of those in your next post.

What I did say (and where we disagree) is that Mint & Ubuntu should give credit where credit is due.  No Debian means no Ubuntu, no Ubuntu means no Mint.  I don't see how this is a problematic statement.  I guess you think that there is no obligation to acknowledge all of the work done by others.  I do.   I know Mint and its users doesn't owe me anything I am not Ubuntu nor am I Debian.

Why do you insist on presenting the mere acknowledgment of the work of others as some sort of upper/lower relationship?  As if there is some shame in saying "our work is possible due to your work".  In fairness to the Mint developers, *they do acknowledge the relationship between Ubuntu & Mint*.   I'm not saying that Mint or any distro has to have some big welcome screen when you log in thanking every coder F/OSS contributor from Ubuntu all the way back to Linus & Richard.  But a mention on the website or in the "About DistroX" concerning how your work depends on the work of others isn't an outlandish thing to do.

I'm happy that you did not encounter problems with Mint, but I can pull anecdotal stories from people for many distros where DistroX was better OOTB than DistroY, that's part of the beauty of the Linux/BSD ecosystem.  If 1 distro doesn't work you have options.

Your bit about about Microsoft...are you serious?  Let me answer your question with a question:  What's so wrong with someone who uses linux today acknowledging that their first use of computers was on DOS, or Windows, or Mac or OS/2 or Unix or whatever other non-linux OS?  

If someone asks me "rs3york  what was your first experience with computers?" I'll say DOS & Windows 3.1.  That's simply stating facts, and I'm not sending some debt to Redmond by stating those facts.  That you would have used a different OS if Microsoft didn't exist is irrelevant because you *did* use Windows, full-stop.  I'm not in error for being grateful to my school teachers because 'if they weren't alive, somebody else would have been my teacher' nor does that mean I "bow down" to them.  

I did not claim that the Mint devs have not improved Ubuntu.  What I did say is that the degree of changes made by the Mint devs on top of Ubuntu is less than the degree of changes made by the Ubuntu devs on top of Debian.  As for art & looks, they are important.  But they are also subjective and easily changed, you can throw the art of one distro on to another or use your own and have a totally unique custom desktop.  Looks aren't everything, I thought Fedora 8 was beautiful but I didn't like it as a distro so I don't use it anymore. I'll say it again, if you want to reject my statements with some facts to the contrary I'll happily take the corrections.

> 
Again stop taking pride that Mint copies us, they use our repositories, big deal. ... I like Debian and have used it for some time, but I really don't think Ubuntu owes them anything. Ubuntu has improved what Debian made and Mint has improved what Ubuntu made.

I don't think we should look down on Mint users that "ohh u rely on us for ur updates, hahahah". Thats just how Apple says Microsoft relies on them for innovation. 


First off, the claim that MS relies on Apple for innovation is quite different than the fact that Mint relies on Ubuntu for updates & software.  As it stands now, MS could innovate without Apple (and they do), in comparison you cannot install much additional software or get too many updates (security or otherwise) in a Mint Linux install without the Ubuntu repositories.  That is unless you want to try and see if Debian repos will work or compile everything from source.  **It is not about "pride".  It is not about "looking down" on anybody.  It IS about acknowledging the relationship. Would you be "looking down" at your friend by saying 'thank you' if she drove you to work everyday?  And would she be unreasonable for thinking it would be nice to be acknowledged?  Even if you think you are better at your job than she is at hers?**

In fact I find it interesting that you push so hard for this 'Mint is not Ubuntu and Mint doesn't owe Ubuntu anything like Ubuntu doesn't owe Debian anything' angle yet you acknowledge:
1)"that Mint copies [ubuntu]" {original "us"}
2)"they use [ubuntu] repositories" {original "our"}
3)"Ubuntu has improved what Debian made and Mint has improved what Ubuntu made"

Even you acknowledge those points, so what's so controversial about the projects acknowledging them too?

As for internet speeds, of course it's nice to have less to download.  Nobody is disputing that.  But the size of the codecs is not huge, certainly not like downloading another ISO.  *That doesn't make more downloading preferable but we should put it in context.*  Further if you don't have internet at all or a have a slow connection...beyond MP3s & other audio (such as RealMedia) where are you pulling down the content that needs the codecs in the first place? The H.264 HD Video?  Those big XviD/DivX files?  The Matroska files with surround sound and multiple languages audio streams?  If you have a friend who has a fast connection and brings CDs/DVDs or an external hard drive over with content, that same friend could bring you the deb files for codecs.  Only a niche of people who encode content benefit here and even then Mint doesn't include all the software they would need to be productive so they would need an internet connection (and patience to wait) anyway.

> 
There r many people on these forums for who Mint 4.0 works but Ubuntu 7.10 doesn't. Mint developers must be doing something that Ubuntu developers can't right?


Wrong.  

First off, Mint is based upon Ubuntu and releases after Ubuntu.  If Product B is based on Product A and Product B is released after Product A, any reasonable person would expect Product B to have improvements.

Second, what Mint does after Ubuntu is not a reflection on what the Ubuntu devs are able to do.  Some things aren't done by Ubuntu for philosophical/legal reasons (such as codecs) others are going into a later version.  Still others are not a priority for Ubuntu devs or even relevant/necessary to Ubuntu (like MintInstall for example).

Third, the improvements made by the Mint developers are only possible because Debian & Ubuntu developers already took care of the bulk of the work!  The same is true for Ubuntu devs being able to do certain things because Debian devs took care of the bulk of underlying work for them.  Mint devs have the advantage of leveraging the Debian & Ubuntu communities for the vast majority of work then adding their own improvements.

### Post by blu3ness on 2008-01-01
I have used Mint on my laptop for the past year, I have also used Ubuntu, PCLinuxOS before that, but in my opinion:

Mint is still largely a community driven distro, while Ubuntu has financial and commercial backing, that alone makes a huge difference. Mint advantages in area of codec and little extra gimmicks are very nice for casual users, but irrelevant over the long run. (I have turned off all the mint gimmicks on my laptop, the vista-esque start menu alone takes up 24mb of memory if you install some extra applications). You can even transform Ubuntu into mint by just adding their repositories and installing a few packages. Mint is really just ubuntu, it's just more tuned and adjusted for the lazy people.

If you look at the release schedules of mint and ubuntu, you'll see a pattern that's quite self-explainatory. Mint are always released about 2~3 months after an Ubuntu release.  So yeah, they ARE teh same.

On the other hand... I'm ever more tempted to try ArchLinux after reading more and more about it..

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-02
> **snakeeyes said:**
>  Ok, Ubuntu really isn't the most high profile distro. I had heard about Suse 4 years before I heard about Ubuntu.  Duh. SUSE is way older than Ubuntu. Of course you have heard about SUSE *before* Ubuntu. You can't hear about a distro if it doesn't exist yet, right? :)  I used SUSE when I started with Linux back in 1996 but since 2004 abandoned it when Ubuntu showed up. Ubuntu got better and better whereas SUSE got worse and worse.

> **snakeeyes said:**
>  Saying that Mint is just a flashy version of Ubuntu  Mint = Ubuntu + custom kernel (slightly modified default setup) + a few extra packages written by the Mint community + a few extra wallpapers created by the Mint community + a new package manager called "mint Installer" (that works with their "Mint Software Portal" and the *.mint software packages there) + a few extra Mint repos where the "Mint" version of some software packages are kept (e.g. their customized version of Firefox) + codecs "out of the box" (some of which are illegal in the USA due to copyright laws there).

That's it. That's the whole magic. Don't get me wrong: I find what "Clem" (the creator of Mint) did is admirable; but "Mint" isn't soooooo different from Ubuntu as you are trying to present it to be. And yes, I am qualified to say that (2299 mostly support-related postings on the Mint forum), I know Mint pretty well inside-out.

> **snakeeyes said:**
>  As for the codecs ... I get sick of the fact that now I have to download codecs as well.  True. And most countries and territories on this planet don't even have any laws similar to the US "DMCA" or similar laws, e.g. software patents aren't worth anything in most countries. So shipping a Linux distro with the codecs --even "problematic" ones-- pre-installed is perfectly legal outside the USA. That's precisely what the Mint people do (they are Germany + Ireland based). So I find it strange that Ubuntu which is based in South Africa(?) tries to play "nice" according to US laws which even don't apply in South Africa or most of the rest of this planet. That's something where I think that Mint did it right.

> **snakeeyes said:**
>   that Mint relies on Ubuntu and stuff.  Some people seem to forget that this is *precisely* **_the_** idea of open source and the GPL. Mint is based on Ubuntu. So what? That's exactly the idea here and of the GPL and Linux in general. And if anyone feels like creating even a better distro based on Mint .... Nice! Do it! Go ahead. Thanks to the GPL *everyone* will benefit from your improvements. That's what this GPL stuff is all about. You are supposed to be able to base your stuff on someone else's stuff if you want to do exactly that, provided that everything was covered by the GPL (or a similar license which allows for this kind of source code copying).

---

### Post by molom on 2008-01-03
Hi everyone,
This is a small video stating some few features and wallpapers of the Linux Mint E17 edition coming out January 2008. There will be a CD & DVD edition. The CD edition is designed for slow/outdated computers, thus has minimal apps like mplayer, Firefox, Thunderbird, XMMS and so on. While the DVD edition is designed for decent or new computers and will include very high quality apps. Both editions should be available during January 2008.


What is so good about Linux Mint E17 compared to other OS's:
   - All codecs are preinstalled to give an excellent 'out of the box' experience
   - The art is designed for many people, there is a light theme, a dark theme and a minimalistic theme
   - It has a minimum requirement of 64mb of ram
   - It has plenty of eyecandy without sacrificing much CPU usage
   - There is a CD version and DVD version
   - Already comes with apps like Inkscape, GIMP, Jokosher and more
   - It has one of the fastest modern DE's
   - Isn't very cluttered
   - And is elegant

If you weren't happy with elive, gOS, Geubuntu, OZOS, Yellow Dog Linux or elbuntu, Linux Mint E17 might be the one just for you.

### Post by Antman on 2008-01-03
> **juxtaposed said:**
> Heh. I'll stick with Debian.

I just found out that Linux Mint has a technological preview of how it would work with Debian Testing instead of a Ubuntu base.

> Release Notes for Linux Mint 4.0 Daryna Debian Edition ALPHA 023

This release is an ALPHA release. It is not intented to be used as your main operating system but to give you a technological preview of how Linux Mint would behave if it was based on Debian Testing.

Debian is a rolling distribution. Compared to Ubuntu, Debian would allow Linux Mint to be faster and to feel snappier and it would ease the process of migrating from one release to another since the Debian base would constantly get updated underneath the Linux Mint desktop.

Debian Testing is unfortunately also less stable than Ubuntu and lacks polish in certain areas.

This experimentation shows us that:

    * It is possible for Linux Mint to easily switch base (if Ubuntu disappeared or went in a different way)
    * A Debian Testing base would make Linux Mint faster.
    * A Debian Testing base would let users transparently go from one release to another by simply applying updates in mintUpdate (release would be like simple snapshots in time)
    * A Debian Testing base would be easier to maintain in the long term for the Linux Mint dev team
    * A Debian Testing base would not always be stable, and this would represent temporary problems for both developers and users.
    * A Debian Testing base would lack the many innovations and improvements developped by the Ubuntu developers, putting more pressure on the Linux Mint team to achieve the same level of polish.

The interesting thing about this experiment will be to see if it still behaves fine in 6 or 12 months time.

In the meantime, you can have a look at it. The ISO acts as a non-installable live CD which you can boot on your computer.


Sounds very interesting...

---

### Post by rainwalker on 2008-01-03
I just tried Mint (GNOME version) a little while ago and I don't see what the fuss is about.

It didn't detect my wireless card, which Ubuntu has recognized instantly since Edgy.
It didn't let me enable visual effects, which I have been able to do just fine since Edgy (and it was using the same driver I am).
I thought a lot of the icons were kind of ugly, which was kind of a letdown since their motto is "From freedom came elegance".
Codecs are no big deal for me, because they're easy enough to install on Ubuntu.

However, I did like a few things about it;
The wallpaper was nice. Better than the default Ubuntu ones, except maybe Dawn Of Ubuntu from way back on Dapper.
Simple workarounds for useful things, like having certain icons showing on the desktop (trash, home folder, etc.) which aren't obvious in Ubuntu.
I saw something in it's Control Center about a utility for loading Windows wireless drivers, which (assuming it works) would be nice.

I'm definitely sticking with Ubuntu; the only advantage I see with Mint is the inclusion of codecs (which is really just one less hassle, not really a big deal to me).

### Post by Yannick Le Saint kyncani on 2008-01-04
Hi, I was wondering what this mint distro was.

Now according to [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linux_Mint](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linux_Mint) , linux mint does have an update-manager -like application called MintUpdate that's supposed to address "the insecurity behind Ubuntu's Update Manager" (quoting wikipedia here).

I was wondering what mint did better than ubuntu and decided to take a look at MintUpdate's source code.

Going through mint's website [http://www.linuxmint.com/index.html](http://www.linuxmint.com/index.html) I have not found the source code for MintUpdate. At the bottom of the page is written "If you can't find what you're looking for please write to root AT linuxmint DOT com and we'll provide the source to you".

In ubuntu, the source code for update-manager is easily available in [http://packages.ubuntu.com/gutsy/gnome/update-manager](http://packages.ubuntu.com/gutsy/gnome/update-manager) .

I don't think I'm going to use a distro that does not make source code easily available any time soon ...

### Post by exploder on 2008-01-05
This is "Other OS", it is the proper place for this kind of discussion. Fortunately, many people see Linux as Linux and are happy to help with trouble shooting  problems regardless of what distribution is being used.

I tend to ask questions here because this is such a large and friendly forum and there are some very knowledgeable and talented people here.

Ubuntu has given assistance to other distributions as far as providing source code so Ubuntu based distributions can comply with the GPL. Mepis received such help when it was Ubuntu based and I am sure other distributions have received similar assistance 

Ubuntu uses Debian code, Mint uses Ubuntu code and both use technology developed by other distributions. Pulse Audio is a good example, it's development is now coming from Fedora. Ubuntu is adopting Pulse Audio for Hardy. Everyone benefits by the sharing of information  and technology. This is what Linux is all about!

The Ubuntu forum has this section especially for this kind of discussion so obviously the Ubuntu Community does not have a problem with discussing other distributions. The Ubuntu Community provided the means for this kind of discussions to take place. 

So, PriceChild, ther is nothing unfair here. Linux is Linux and the Ubuntu Community is very generous in providing a place to discuss "Other Operating Systems".

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-05
> **r4ik said:**
> i really don't see why any user, Mint or whatever can't visit for support. There are various changes in Mint which more and more make it different from Ubuntu; the same way that Ubuntu is different from Debian. E.g. Mint uses some own version numbers in some packages which may cause conflicts with "apt". Those problems are not present in Ubuntu. Those increasing numbers of incompatibilities that Mint has makes it difficult from the support POV; especially if you assume that they are using Ubuntu and those users don't mention the many many differences their Mint has ... You'll run into many unexpected problems which will be more and more difficult to reproduce.

Just go to a Debian support site ... they will tell you the same about your Ubuntu installation.

---

---

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-05
> **wolfen69 said:**
> please give specific examples. 
[http://linuxmint.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=47&t=423&p=2421&#p2216](http://linuxmint.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=47&t=423&p=2421&#p2216)

Please feel free to use the search functions or Google in the future. Or just believe what I tell you. There is no gain for me in lying to you ... So you could just as well assume that I am always telling the truth. :)

As you can see from that thread there this was about a problem in Linux Mint 2.1 (released late December 2006). This release all of a sudden introduced some Mint-specific version numbers whereas the previous release Mint 2.0 "Barbara" strictly sticked to Ubuntu's version numbers. So here in that thread we're dealing with broken dependencies in the **libpanel-applet2-0** package (which is needed for Gnome) and we were facing problems because Ubuntu's version back then was **"[COLOR="Blue"]2.16.1-0ubuntu4~prop1[/COLOR]"** but Mint introduced a new version **"[COLOR="Red"]2.16.1-1linuxmint1[/COLOR]"** ... and this was enough to break dependencies and make matters very very complicated.

As you can see from that thread it took me a while to figure the problem out back then.

Since then a little more than a year has passed. And even more packages in the current Mint releases now have their own Mint-specific version numbers (e.g. Mint uses its own version of Firefox), thus more and more breaking compatibility with Ubuntu's repos.

Happy with my answer? :)

---

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-05
> **PriceChild said:**
> As long as they state it explicitly. Even if ... the more they progress the more Mint will do things different than Ubuntu. E.g. already now they have their own Mint-specific tools to deal with NTFS drives (mintdisk) and WiFi connections (mintwifi) and all those tools have their own config files. If you just use Ubuntu you won't know about those Mint specialties ... So maybe you will be able to help a Mint user and give advice that works for them too ... Or maybe it won't work because whatever advice you gave just broke something else on their system. So Mint users coming here and asking for help ... Yes, it probably works OK right now, it may still work with the next Mint release for some basic stuff ... but sooner or later Mint will have moved quite a bit away from Ubuntu. And as soon as that gap is too big there won't be a point for Mint users coming here because Ubuntu's forum staff people and users simply won't be able to help anymore .... IMHO.

Did you know that they are working on their own package management system? "Mint Software Portal" from where you download *.mint packages and then install them via "mintinstall" .... As I said, the gap is widening.

### Post by blendenzo on 2008-01-06
Well, it seems there's a lot of back-and-forth in this thread, so I might as well contribute to it ;)

I've been using MInt since 2.1.  I've compared each Mint version to the corresponding Ubuntu version, and I have always liked Mint better.  That's my opinion, and I hope someone finds it useful.  Currently, I use Mint 4.0, and it works well for me.

One nice touch I noticed when I updated my system to Mint 4.0 was the inclusion of GIMP 2.4 (RC3) as opposed to the standard GIMP 2.2 that was in the Ubuntu/Debian repositories at the time.  (Ubuntu has since caught up.)

Mint is a lot like Ubuntu, true.  But it is becoming more and more its own distro with each release.  These things take time.  I for one like the direction Mint is taking, and for now I'm sticking with it.  Also, the Mint Debian idea sounds really interesting to me.  I hope that they continue to pursue it.

BTW, I've successfully installed the Mint 3.1 standard install on two laptops with ~500Mhz CPUs and 192 MB RAM (one is a Compaq Presario 1600, which I have since upgraded to Mint 4.0, and the other is a Dell Latitude of some sort).  It runs rather fast on both considering the specs (though I do have to quit MintUpdate at every boot because it takes up 30-90 MB RAM... haven't taken the time to disable it from startup yet).

---

### Post by exploder on 2008-01-06
Scorp123 is correct. Mint 4.0 is not directly built from Gutsy. The base system goes back a couple of versions and improvements that are desired are added. The system is actually built! Mint is not Gutsy with codecs and a new look.

Ubuntu is much the same. Ubuntu is not Debian with a different look. Ubuntu Developer's use a Debian Base and build in the features they want.

What it all comes down to in the end is that Linux is Linux. Different distributions have their own ideas and goals and approach them in different ways.

Sometimes using Ubuntu where I have to install the plug-ins myself, the Flash will just grey-out and won't play, other times it will take my browser down.  I have had not one problem with that on openSuSE, Wolvix, Linux Mint, etc. where it is installed with the system.

---

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-07
> **lespaul_rentals said:**
>  There is a huge difference in how well the plug-ins work depending on whether they are pre-installed or user-installed. Your logic "preinstalled codecs are better" is flawed. When done correctly (e.g. by you and/or the package manager and/or the installer script or whatever ...) you should always get the same results, preinstalled or "user-installed" or not. That's kinda the point of a package manager: It should install stuff the same way, no matter when and how.

If done right then it should be the same. e.g. same files, same symbolic links, same config files in all the same locations, regardless whether or not you installed at installation time or added stuff like codecs later on. If you get different results when you install it then it only means that it wasn't done 100% right whereas the installer does it right or that somewhere somehow there are some bugs which prevent you from getting the result you're supposed to get.

---

### Post by wolfen69 on 2008-01-07
you're right. it ***shouldnt*** matter. yet somehow, in my experience, pre-installed seem to run better. and ive done many, many installs.

---

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-01-07
> **wolfen69 said:**
> you're right. it ***shouldnt*** matter. yet somehow, in my experience, pre-installed seem to run better.  In some distros I have seen that they place needed symbolic links in the wrong places so that user accounts added after the installation don't have access to some library files that would enable certain plugins. In such cases you'd often have to manually place symbolic links in all the right places to get things working again.

For as long as us imperfect human beings bother with writing software, software can never be perfect. :)

---

### Post by lespaul_rentals on 2008-01-08
> **scorp123 said:**
> Your logic "preinstalled codecs are better" is flawed. When done correctly (e.g. by you and/or the package manager and/or the installer script or whatever ...) you should always get the same results, preinstalled or "user-installed" or not. That's kinda the point of a package manager: It should install stuff the same way, no matter when and how.

If done right then it should be the same. e.g. same files, same symbolic links, same config files in all the same locations, regardless whether or not you installed at installation time or added stuff like codecs later on. If you get different results when you install it then it only means that it wasn't done 100% right whereas the installer does it right or that somewhere somehow there are some bugs which prevent you from getting the result you're supposed to get.

Well, I'm just going from experience.  I've run scripts and used package managers, and every single time the pre-installed works better.

### Post by craigyjack on 2008-01-10
I have recently switched to Mint 4.0 from Ubuntu 7.10 and I have to say that I am impressed and like Mint! Ubuntu should have done some things that Mint has done - I'm not sure why it didn't but whatever.
For me, Mint seems to boot very quick! It boots quicker than Ubuntu did. The customized GRUB boot screen is awesome. I like MintMenu - even if I don't use it much since I use avant window navigator :)

Envy came with Mint, so I decided to use it - I was impressed, it installed the latest nvidia drivers flawlessly. I know how to install the drivers manually, I did in Ubuntu because Envy would never work for me in Ubuntu (crashed xserver afterwards), but I ran into many a crashed xserver in Ubuntu because of the drivers. Not ubuntu's fault, but I like having the latest drivers which Ubuntu's restricted driver manager does not have. So Envy working nicely in Mint was great!, no manual nvidia drivers for me now.

MintConfig is very nice! I seriously wondered why Ubuntu never made a central control place for the longest time (like mac's system preferences or window's control panel) It makes more organized - VERY helpful for a linux newbie because there is alot of configs for linux (that is a great thing of course, but organizing it is important). It looks very similiar to Compiz Config settings manager. mintDesktop is also a nice little tool that makes my life a little simpler since I used to do this in gconf-editor. I hope more GUI's are made for gconf configs that are commonly used because gconf is the easiest or prettiest thing to use.

The one thing I did not like in mintInstall. Many have praised it, but I thought it was a pretty pointless peice of crap really, to be honest. The actual GUI is just a simple Browse or Search - and then it just takes you to the software portal webpage....  They removed the very nice gnome-app-install (Add/Remove Apps program) for this! mintInstall website provided some apps that werent in the regular repos, but I found that they were not the latest editions of the software - so I much rather prefer to just go download the latest software from the software's website - usually a .deb which is easy to install! I don't see a need in mintInstall, gnome-app-install does a beautiful job, and I think if Mint wanted to do something about installing apps they should have tweaked gnome-app-install to include some other apps - just make some deb packages for common apps, and integrate it into the nice gnome-app-install. I don't understand why they went the route they did, and tossed gnome-app-install. They should've improved upon gnome-app-install instaed (or kde's install for the kde version). But I have uninstalled mintInstall and installed gnome-app-install again, and made it so that mintMenu opens gnome-app-install to install apps instead of mintInstall :)

Overall, Linux Mint 4.0 is a very nice upgrade from Ubuntu 7.10! I hope Ubuntu sees what Mint is doing and maybe learns a little from them and does some stuff for Hardy Heron. If not, then I'll look forward to Mint 5.0 (which will be based of Hardy Heron)

---

### Post by Ripfox on 2008-01-12
> **finferflu said:**
> I have two things to say. 
The first is about Free Software. What Stallman says is that we shouldn't use proprietary software in order to discourage its propagation. So what distros like Mint are doing is spreading more and more proprietary software, hindering acceptance of open standards. For example, if the vast majority of people keep using MP3, Ogg will never spread so much to take over it. 
On the other side I have to say that we don't have really appetible open source alternatives, as far as I can see. And I don't mean, for example, Gnash vs. Flash, I mean something that does what Flash does, but better. So while I respect the Free Software philosophy I am disappointed with the current results of open source alternatives, but I hope in the future I will be able to completely switch to Free Software. 

Secondly, I have to say that for some people the real fun is in customising the distro to the very detail. I guess this does not really apply to most of the Ubuntu users, but I enjoyed tweaking and trying to make things work, and eventually switched to Arch, which gives me more space for customisation. 

I might try Mint out of curiosity anyway, I love to see how pre-packaged distros work out of the box.

+1 If I want people to install codecs for me I will switch to something like this, but that will never happen.

### Post by gn2 on 2008-01-24
Mint is very similar to Ubuntu, but currently only 32-bit is available and there's no "Alternate CD"

It's a good choice as a first distro.

Surprised you didn't get videos working, you just need to run: 

```
sudo apt-get install ubuntu-restricted-extras
```

Or: 

```
sudo apt-get install xubuntu-restricted-extras
```

Or

```
sudo apt-get install kubuntu-restricted-extras
```

Or if you're using 64-bit read [this]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=476924").

---

### Post by kleo skywalker on 2008-01-24
> **barbedsaber said:**
> so, which desktop enviroment does mint run, and could my grandma use it?

The official one uses GNOME, and there are several other community ones such as KDE and Fluxbox. I installed the GNOME one.

If you think Ubuntu is easy to use, then (so far) I've found Mint to be way easier. So yes, I think your grandma could use it if you install it for her. (I would change the default music player to Exaile though, it's simpler than Amarok and doesn't crash as much.)
Edit: About your grandma - if she's used to Ubuntu and comfortable with it and it's set up to work for her, then there's no particular reason to switch to Mint.

### Post by kleo skywalker on 2008-01-24
Frostwire is still stuck at "Starting Connection". In Ubuntu (Gutsy and Feisty), it connected within seconds. Any ideas why this is happening and how I could fix it?

---

### Post by billgoldberg on 2008-01-24
> **kleo skywalker said:**
> Frostwire is still stuck at "Starting Connection". In Ubuntu (Gutsy and Feisty), it connected within seconds. Any ideas why this is happening and how I could fix it?

Yes, this is a common frostwire glitch.

These instructions should fix it:

1. Go to the terminal

    sudo nautilus

2. Browse to gnutella.net

    /home/[USERNAME]/.frostwire/gnutella.net

( press ctrl+h to see the hidden files)

3. Open gnutella.net (or create it if it doesn’t exist)

4. Delete everything inside the file

5. Replace it with

```
http://mc3.electronicbox.net/gnutella.net
```

6. Save file and restart Frostwire.

[source]("http://linuxowns.wordpress.com/2007/12/11/frostwire/")

---

### Post by TheOrangePeanut on 2008-01-24
I use Mint as my primary OS.  It really isn't that much different from Ubuntu except I didn't have to install the codecs (which is good, because even after I installed them in Ubuntu I couldn't get things like streaming videos to play) and it is prettier, which I'm also down with.  It also came with Amarok instead of Rhythmbox and worked with my iPod without screwing around a lot.  All in all, I prefer it as a "get up and go" distro.

### Post by kleo skywalker on 2008-01-24

> Well, what have you gathered? Why don't you share it in more details then just 'I like it'? Can you also provide links to videos you could not play, I want to try and test it.

Umm... I just searched for threads on Mint on this forum - that's how I gathered whatever information I did. You could try the same. [This very long thread in particular]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=299756").
As for the details - what kind of details do you want?! I said "I like it" because that's what I wanted to say. ### Post by mikewhatever on 2008-01-24
> **kleo skywalker said:**
> Umm... I just searched for threads on Mint on this forum - that's how I gathered whatever information I did. You could try the same. [This very long thread in particular]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=299756").
As for the details - what kind of details do you want?! I said "I like it" because that's what I wanted to say. There is no *information* about Mint that I can share that's not already out there (Wikipedia, Google search, Forums etc.); I started this thread to post about *experiences* with Mint - particularly problems.

'I like it' is perfectly fine, but I was asking about this quote: 'I know little, but from what I've gathered, Mint is more than a further prettyfied Ubuntu with codecs'. To rephrase the the question, what makes you think 'Mint is more'...? I've played with Mint in the past and read several reviews, so here's my take on what's different from Ubuntu, and what may appeal to new (Windows) users.

1.Proprietary codecs, including DVD, are pre-installed. (This may come as a good surprise compaired to Windows, because my XP OEM installation could not play anything but Windows formats.)
2.Proprietary software such as Java and Skype is pre-installed.
3.Only one, bottom panel is used, which may seem more familiar for Windows users.
4.There is a Download Portal to download and install programs just like one would in Windows.
5.Updates are colored showing how dangerous they might be to install.
6.Terminal Window shows surprising sentences some may think are cute.

I've avoided mentioning looks, colors, themes and wallpapers on purpose, because they are easily changed and therefore do not matter much. I may have missed some features, so you're welcome to add more. None of the above, IMO, makes Mint better then Ubuntu, although it is a good distro. What I was after were facts and features in Mint that appeal to users, and I thought you said you had some. Oh well, never mind.

PS:quicktime movies from the link you posted work fine in my Firefox.

PSS:I've looked around on their home site and found no notion of Mint being based on Ubuntu. Is that no longer the case? Here's what 'About Linux Mint' section says:
> Distrowatch:Originally launched as a variant of Ubuntu with integrated media codecs, it has now developed into one of the most user-friendly distributions on the market - complete
> It is a Debian-based distribution and as such it is very solid and it comes with one of the greatest package managers.
Here's what really is on Distowatch: [http://distrowatch.com/table.php?distribution=mint](http://distrowatch.com/table.php?distribution=mint)
> Linux Mint is an Ubuntu-based distribution whose goal is to provide a more complete out-of-the-box experience by including browser plugins, media codecs, support for DVD playback, Java and other components. It also adds a custom desktop and menus, several unique configuration tools, and a web-based package installation interface. Linux Mint is compatible with Ubuntu software repositories.
Does this imply Mint is now based on Debian?

PSS: Welcome to the megathread, everyone. :)

### Post by crjackson on 2008-01-25
> **kleo skywalker said:**
> Really? Could you tell me how you got them playing? Because I tried a whole bunch of things - different how-to's I found on threads here but couldn't get them playing.

On all my basic installs the first thing I do is install this (and install the restricted extras 1st - which is redundant, but that's how I do it).

```
sudo wget http://www.medibuntu.org/sources.list.d/gutsy.list -O /etc/apt/sources.list.d/medibuntu.list
```

```
wget -q http://packages.medibuntu.org/medibuntu-key.gpg -O- | sudo apt-key add - && sudo apt-get update
```

```
sudo apt-get install libdvdcss2 w32codecs gxine totem-xine xine-ui libdmx1 libxine1 libxine1-ffmpeg libxinerama1 totem libdvdread3
```

After that, all I did was go to the page and it just worked...

### Post by jrusso2 on 2008-05-13
It sounds awesome.  Again its Ubuntu done right.  I especially like this part in the release notes as this is the suggestion i had made long time ago for the LTS releases.

Linux Mint 5 Elyssa is built on top of the Ubuntu Hardy Heron package base. Hardy is a Long Term Support (LTS) release, meaning that it will be supported and it will receive security and package updates for the next 3 years.

Starting with Elyssa, Linux Mint will consider two of its releases "current": The latest release, and the latest LTS release. Basically, this means that innovations put into Linux Mint 6, Linux Mint 7 and Linux Mint 8 will be backported into Linux Mint 5 until the next LTS release comes out. Users will have a choice to stay current over the next 2 years by keeping Linux Mint 5 Elyssa, or by following the latest releases every 6 months.

Although Linux Mint isn't a rolling distribution an LTS strategy will be put in place to ensure that Linux Mint 5 Elyssa will stay up to date over the next 2 years. Users will have the choice to enable the backport repository for Elyssa in which upgrades for important desktop applications (Firefox, Thunderbird, OpenOffice..) will be made available.

### Post by Girindor on 2008-05-15
> **pelle.k said:**
> That is your oppinion and i'm not sure everyone shares your view on that. Just thought i'd stress that. It *is* a very good distro though.

I've been with ubuntu since breezy badger, and let me tell you that everyone doesn't necessarily *want* ubuntu to be any more "user friendly" (the way linux mint does it) because a lot of us might not think that linux mint *is* particulary "user friendly". That is why linux mint *and* ubuntu exists. Why should ubuntu do the same thing when there are obviously people who want thing done in different ways. As i see it, it's a win-win
situation with linux mint doing it's own thing and ubuntu *doesn't*.
Do you see what i mean?

Of course there's room for both, I wasn't suggesting that Ubuntu becomes Linux Mint. But there are so many little things that make Mint so much user friendlier for new users. To name a few:
1. There is a menu item to help you deal with proprietary graphics cards (Mint does not come with proprietary components, but enables you to easily get your graphics card, for instance, to work).
2. In the same vein, there's a menu item for using Windows Wireless drivers (i.e. ndiswrapper)
3. You can right click on an application in the menu and uninstall etc. This is really intuitive and beyond a doubt makes things more user-friendly.
4. It makes it easier to install software than in Ubuntu. You and I might think it's already easy enough, but your Grandma might not agree, and more to the point - people who are used to Windows like to click on something in the internet (download and install an executable). Of course, in Ubuntu you can do that with .deb files, but in Mint, from the same menu, you can chose to search for programs through apt, GetDeb.net, or the Mint software portal (.mint files). They are really working on integrating CNR.com support, something which is available for Ubuntu but how would anyone know if it isn't integrated?

I could go on here for a while, but - I love Ubuntu and I agree it should do things differently, otherwise what's the point of having distros. BUT, given it's purpose of being "Linux for human beings", I think there are loads of tiny, smart, ideas, that would make a *huge* difference to people trying it for the first time.

---

### Post by exploder on 2008-06-09
There are a lot of differences and not just the codecs. The install process is different, no Wubi or languages at the start of the Live CD. The menu has loads of features and can be customized very easily. The artwork is some of the best and Mint even has it's own artwork site now!

[http://www.linuxmint-art.org/](http://www.linuxmint-art.org/)

The Software Portal keeps getting better and better. Mint 5 is the only release I know of that comes with Firefox 3 RC1 and Flash 10 beta by default. All of the Pulse Audio packages are installed by default.

The default start page for Firefox now displays the latest news for LinuxMint. All of the Mint tools now blend in perfectly with Gnome. I personally like that mintdesktop has the ability to restore splash screens! 

Mint has quite a few differences these days!

### Post by 67GTA on 2008-06-09
Something most people don't know is that Mint is based on Ubuntu Edgy(has been since Bianca). Clem doesn't just remaster each new Ubuntu version and add codecs to it. It uses each previous Mint base, and all packages are updated on top of that. Then all the Mint tweaks and apps are added. It is not a Ubuntu clone.

### Post by Rui Pais on 2008-06-10
> **67GTA said:**
> Something most people don't know is that Mint is based on Ubuntu Edgy(has been since Bianca). Clem doesn't just remaster each new Ubuntu version and add codecs to it. It uses Edgy's base, and all packages are updated on top of that. Then all the Mint tweaks and apps are added. It is not a Ubuntu clone.

From here:
[http://www.linuxmint.com/rel_elyssa.php](http://www.linuxmint.com/rel_elyssa.php)

Quote from the "New Feature" #12:
> 
12. LTS aspects

**Linux Mint 5 Elyssa is built on top of the Ubuntu Hardy Hero**n package base. Hardy is a Long Term Support (LTS) release, meaning that it will be supported and it will receive security and package updates for the next 3 years.

Starting with Elyssa, Linux Mint will consider two of its releases "current": The latest release, and the latest LTS release. Basically, this means that innovations put into Linux Mint 6, Linux Mint 7 and Linux Mint 8 will be backported into Linux Mint 5 until the next LTS release comes out. Users will have a choice to stay current over the next 2 years by keeping Linux Mint 5 Elyssa, or by following the latest releases every 6 months.

Although Linux Mint isn't a rolling distribution an LTS strategy will be put in place to ensure that Linux Mint 5 Elyssa will stay up to date over the next 2 years. Users will have the choice to enable the backport repository for Elyssa in which upgrades for important desktop applications (Firefox, Thunderbird, OpenOffice..) will be made available.

---

### Post by 67GTA on 2008-06-10
I think Clem is mincing his words. It is compatible with Hardy, because it uses some of it's repos. Elyssa is not based on Hardy. It is based on Daryna, which was based on, Celena, etc. The packages that Mint shares with Ubuntu are upgraded to Hardy's version. I think the words need to be a little clearer. The introduction has it exactly right. It is only sharing some of the Hardy repos, so it has to be upgraded to have the same version of packages as Hardy.

### Post by knavarathna92 on 2008-06-24
> **LaRoza said:**
> Beryl isn't active. I think Mint comes with all the Ubuntu things +, so I imagine it would be similiar. 

For compiz, you would just install the settings manager and the plugins, then customize it the usual way.

Mint probably has the same package managers.

well, Mint already has advanced settings/ simple settings manager installed.  I believe what this guy did was go simple config and set the effects level to "Hollywood got nothing" then used advanced settings manager to make some changes.  Advanced manager is easy to use, you'll be able to figure it out.  To go to simple config manager, simply right click your desktop, go to change background, go to appearance tab then look at preferences for custom.

Mint uses the same package manager but it also has a software portal with .mint files which contain normally difficult to install programs on ubuntu but have a simplified install with .mint 

P.S. to uninstall an app, right click it in the menu then select uninstall.  Like this feature a lot.

### Post by angry_johnnie on 2008-06-25
> **phoenixankit said:**
> How did he get that animated wallpaper? That was brilliant.

?

You mean those flying colored spots?

I think that would be the fireflies plugin.
Somewhere in the forums, there's a thread on how to get 13 extra compiz plugins, some of them really god... and some... just not that good. :-)

That was a gutsy thread, I think :confused: and some of those plugins are already available in hardy heron --3d windows, for example-- but some others didn't make it (like the fireflies plugin).

I guess the first thing to do would be to open synaptic and search for compiz-plugins.

You should see compiz-plugins-extra and maybe compiz-plugins-unsupported.  Install those, if you don't already have them, to get all the effects.

Also, look for compizconfig-settings-manager, if you don't already have it.  That's where you change the settings.

While I was writing, I found [this]("http://forum.compiz-fusion.org/showthread.php?p=56851"), which is the guide to get all the other plugins.

If you feel adventurous, that's the way to do it. :-)

### Post by djkmmo on 2008-07-17
> **gn2 said:**
> There are no Xfce or KDE versions of Mint5, there's no 64-bit version.

That's not entirely true. You can install both XFCE and KDE, as well as kubuntu-desktop, ubuntu-desktop and xubuntu-desktop on the basis of a stock Mint install. All the packages available for K/X/Ubuntu-users are also available for Mint users, as the repositories are the same + some extra for the Mint stuff (update tool etc.). As a matter of fact, these extra stuff amounts to 17 items, the rest is Medi/X/K/Ubuntu.

The reason why XFCE runs faster on Mint, as been pointed out elsewhere on the forum, might be that those people might have installed the XFCE package rather than the xubuntu-desktop package.

---

### Post by djkmmo on 2008-07-17

OK, so my Jaguar are in fact entirelly different as compared to those sold in the UK, as it's LHD!? I beg to differ. Not all Vauxhall are/were RHD either. We had a Vauxhall a long time ago and it was, naturally, LHD.

There are indeed differences between Ubuntu and Linux Mint, but those differences are far less substantial then the differences between e.g. Ubuntu and Xubunt or Kubuntu, I would say. All you have to to transform a Mint install to a stock Ubuntu is to disable the Mint repositories, unistall the Mint menu, update tool et cetera, install ubuntu-desktop and Bob's your uncle. You could of course go the other way around to, should you want to.

The point is that there's no point at all for Ubuntu users to switch to Mint to "try something different". Should one want the update tool, just add the Mint repositories as source and install it! Fancy another theme? Well just fetch it and install it trough Synaptics/Aptitude in Ubuntu.

### Post by Lostincyberspace on 2008-07-17
What is the difference between Debian and Ubuntu. In most distributions what varies are the package manager, the default applications, and the User Interface. There are minor differences in other areas to, but since linux is open source when one person changes something it eventually gets changed in all.

But I do agree with you that there is virtually no difference besides theme (Which I prefer over ubuntu's) and default applications, every thing else is pretty much the same. Linux Mint is a nice quick and good looking install and work more out of the box than ubuntu does because of the codec and flash already installed.

In the end they are all linux and at the center are pretty much the same just the usability and friendliness to the user changes from distro to distro.

### Post by Zero Prime on 2008-07-18
> **Anurag_panda said:**
> I see your point.
The 32bit Ubuntu users can.
(1) Add Mint repositories
(2) Install mint-desktop package.
(3) Make some modifications.
(4) Voila. Its Mint.

But not applicable for 64bit users though.

Wrong, as are all the posts regarding this.  Mint is not 8.04 with more tools and codecs.  Mint is built on Ubuntu Edgy and every iteration after the original is also based on Edgy.  The core is modified for every new release so that it can use the repos for the newest Ubuntu release.  Linux Mint devs do not take the newest version of Ubuntu and modify it.  This is why Mint runs a lot faster on some computers than Ubuntu.  Edgy was a lot faster for many people, and since Mint is based on Edgy then it is faster.

To make a long story short, you can not make Ubuntu into Mint by adding the repos and tools, nor can you turn Mint into Ubuntu by taking things away.

---

### Post by Robertjm on 2008-07-19

[COLOR="Green"]Linux Mint 5 Elyssa is built on top of the Ubuntu Hardy Heron package base. Hardy is a Long Term Support (LTS) release, meaning that it will be supported and it will receive security and package updates for the next 3 years.
[/COLOR]

### Post by Zero Prime on 2008-07-19
And if you were to dig a little deeper you would find this....
> Ubuntu :)
The main Gnome edition forked off Ubuntu with Barbara which was based on Edgy.
All the versions of the main edition is based on the previous version. Clem uses programming tools and "handicraft" to tweak Mint after the new Ubuntu and of course adds a taste of Mint - the Mint tools and the art work.
This means that it is not exactly the same as the equivalent Ubuntu version, Mint tends to be less buggy and mostly wifi works better. There may also be deliberate differences like in printing where Daryna kept it as in Celena while Gutsy is different from Feisty.
The other important edition is the KDE edition. It is based on Gutsy Kubuntu with the Mint tools (that are gnome) ported to KDE. To make the Mint tools work in KDE was a major undertaking.
The KDE edition will continue to be based on the previous version like the main edition, but here it is Boo that is "tweaking"
And then read on...
> I am curious...if Mint is forked off of Edgy, how can Mint 4.0 be compatible with Gusty? I am just trying to understand how it works.

Thanks...Paul
And then the answer...
> Daryna is based off of Celena and the package base is upgraded to Gutsy's.
Elyssa will be based off of Daryna and upgraded to Hardy's package base.

All new Mint flavors are built on top of the previous, which is based on Edgy.  The code is modified for new releases.

### Post by exploder on 2008-07-19
LinuxMint has a different perspective on what user friendly is. Ubuntu is great, don't get me wrong but Ubuntu is resistant to change in some respects.

Mint uses a single panel layout and the Mint menu. Many people that come from a Windows background do not like a two panel layout.Mint has refined the menu. What other menu allows you to right click and uninstall an application?

The Mint software Portal lets you see a screenshot, read reviews and see a user rating. Many people that are new to Linux have trouble understanding how to install software. LinuxMint attemts to make software installs as painless as possible.

LinuxMint has the irc channel set up by default. This provides quick access to help if needed.

Mint backup makes it simple to back up your Home folder, just a couple of mouse clicks and your done.

The Mint Assistant give you the choice of weather to enable the root account or not. (The default is disabled.) Some people want this choice and it is provided as an option.

LinuxMint provides an elegant look. Many people do not find brown and orange to be very elegant. This is subjective of course but you do see a lot of people on the Ubuntu forums seeking a change.

Both Ubuntu and LinuxMint are excellent choices, the key word is choice.

You could always try Kiwi-Linux...

[http://kiwilinux.org/kiwi/en/](http://kiwilinux.org/kiwi/en/)

There's nothing much to it because KiwiLinux **is Ubuntu** except it comes with various meta-packages linked to codecs and various other semi-closed off software preinstalled for you. (Also, the medibuntu repository enabled by default).

And there is a really cool package included too called restoregrub.

### Post by tiachopvutru on 2008-07-23
> **diego898 said:**
> I need some help with partition planning however. I read a little on psychocats but am still a little confused. I havent installed ubuntu yet. I current lhave a 100gb harddrive with vista taking up about 26 (pretty much out of the box install). I heard it was good practice to create about a 10gb partition for default ubuntu installation, and then keep the rest for a shared partition between vista and ubuntu? (/home partition)?

If you want to go for a simple route, there's no need to plan partition at all. You can just simply create a new partition, install Ubuntu on it, and be done with. However, this solution is limited if you want to access your personal files that are in Ubuntu from Windows.

It's pretty much your choice which scheme you choose. If you want your /home directory (where you put your personal files) to be the shared partition, don't forget to format it as FAT32 or NTFS so that Windows can see them (by default, Ubuntu uses ext3 filesystem, which Windows cannot see).

---

### Post by KhaaL on 2008-07-23
> **diego898 said:**
> I need some help with partition planning however. I read a little on psychocats but am still a little confused. I havent installed ubuntu yet. I current lhave a 100gb harddrive with vista taking up about 26 (pretty much out of the box install). I heard it was good practice to create about a 10gb partition for default ubuntu installation, and then keep the rest for a shared partition between vista and ubuntu? (/home partition)?

I think 10 GB will be fine for your root (ubuntu) partition. then it's recommended to have a seperate home partition aswell. regarding the shared partition, NTFS is a safe bet. ext3 works if you install IFS drivers under windows, but I have experience that it can mess up ownership of the files.

if you want to play safe, you could install it under wubi, that way you won't mess up your partitions and be left with a unbootable PC if something goes haywire.

Good luck :)

### Post by ibuclaw on 2008-07-23
If restricted drivers/out-of-box functionality concern you, you can just stick to the Vanilla Ubuntu. Afterall, there are plenty of howto's.

[The Complete Streaming Thread]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=766683") is probably the most recommended one around for everything from out-of-box flash to DVD-menus.

And as for support for Kiwi. As I said earlier, **Kiwi is Ubuntu**. Ubuntu support is all you need.
All packages [except for a small few]("http://kiwilinux.org/archive/pool/misc/") are from the Ubuntu main/universe repository.

The only notable differences are
[LIST]
[*]Choosing Audacious over Rhythmbox
[*]Different Bootsplash
[*]Different Login Theme
[/LIST]

Nothing else on the surface has changed.

And bodhi.zazen, true.
But Ubuntu still is dependant on Debian, in the aspect that the sid repository is merged every six months... and with Ubuntu, at least the devs try to sync the "**source-code**" back to Debian.

You can't expect two distributions to be 100% binary compatible, but you can make sure, and guarantee that the source-code will always be.

Regards
Iain

### Post by tiachopvutru on 2008-07-24
> **diego898 said:**
> Atallicus you seem to have been in the same boat that I am in now! Im sticking with ubuntu, but how did you manage to get all those things running that mint already comes out of the box running? Simply by installing teh restricted extras package? And why is enlighenment so amazing? (what is it? lol)

As people already said, Linux Mint is Ubuntu with some extra packages (and to be fair to the Linux Mint guys, they say that there are some bug fixes, too).

To get all the necessary multimedia and codecs stuffs, go install the **ubuntu-restricted-extras** metapackage. You will find it in Synaptic Package Manager using the search, or alternatively, you can open the terminal by Applications > Accessories > Terminal, and type in:

```
sudo apt-get install ubuntu-restricted-extras
```

After that, you will also need some stuffs from medibuntu for DVD playback and playing some windows format (not sure about the latter). To do that, look [here]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Medibuntu").

Look under *"Adding the Repositories"* and copy the line under *Ubuntu 8.04 "Hardy Heron"* into terminal and run it. Then scroll down only a little bit, you will see *"Then, add the GPG key."* Copy the code under it into terminal and press Enter. These steps simply add the Medibuntu Repositories so you can get the packages you may need.

Now, to get DVD and Non-Native Format Playback, install **libdvdcss2** and **w32codecs** if you are using the 32-bit version (i386). If you are using the 64-bit version (amd64), install **libdvdcss2** and **w64codecs**. You can find them in synaptic or you can install them using:
```
sudo apt-get package_name
```

Replace **package_name** with the necessary packages you need to install. So, if you want to install **libdvdcss2** and **w32codecs**, do:
```
sudo apt-get install libdvdcss2 w32codecs
```

Now you are pretty much done with the codecs and multimedia stuffs. However, if you are like me, you may not like the default movie player in Ubuntu. I would recommend using **smplayer**. Search for it inside synaptic and install (since I'm not sure if smplayer is the package's name).

For audio player, I would recommend Exaile or Amarok (again, search them in synaptic). For burning utility, use K3b (or the default Ubuntu ones if you like them).

If you want to use Compiz-Fusion, install proprietary drivers as found in the guide in psychocat tutorial [here]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/nvidia") (you can install ati drivers like that, too).


However, if you haven't done that already, I would suggest install Ubuntu now. Chances are, your hardware may not be compatible, and you wouldn't want to use Ubuntu then (hardware incompatibility sucks).

Anyway, enjoy Ubuntu if you hardware does work with it since Ubuntu is good at detecting hardware. :)

---

### Post by tiachopvutru on 2008-07-24
> **Atallicus said:**
> I guess I shouldn't compare Ubuntu to Mint in too many ways.  In mint you get an icon in your try that you click which gives you the restricted driver.  Just wondered if the process was similar.  Doesn't sound too bad.

Yes, it's the same in Ubuntu.

> **diego898 said:**
> Chances are my dell inspiron E1505 has hardware that is incompatible with Ubuntu?

Yes, "chances." But then, chances are everything will work fine. It seems that other users with the same laptops have Ubuntu working, though. If you have trouble with wireless, installing Windows driver using ndiswrapper may help. For now, focus on trying Ubuntu with the LiveCD and installing it.

EDIT: If your wireless device isn't recognized, instead of trying ndiswrapper, check your System > Administration > Hardware Drivers to see if the driver for your wireless device is there (chances are it's there).

### Post by 67GTA on 2008-07-24
Mint is not just Ubuntu with codecs. It split with Edgy. Linux Mint 5 is not a remaster of Hardy. Mint 5 is based on Mint 4, not Hardy. Each version of Mint since Edgy has been based on the previous version of Mint, and updated with the latest Ubuntu package base, just like Ubuntu uses the current Debian sid packages for their latest release. In the next few Mint releases, it will move even farther from Ubuntu.

### Post by molom on 2008-07-25
Another Mint vs Ubuntu page (sigh)
Ok... I'll give my opinion.
Mint is forked off Ubuntu, not fully based, remember that. Some things are different... For example the synaptic is different. 
Mint is slower, and uses python apps=slow
Mint comes with a theme I personally don't like, it looks toyish in my opinion, it isn't sleek and professional, although they now have some nice themes included though. 
Ubuntu, terrific support, mints stuff isn't really needed, All I like is the Mintmenu and mintinstall, but tuxsoftware and USP can replace that and they are better.
My recommendation, stick with Debian, use Parsix, faster, stabler and the same user friendliness.

Cheers

### Post by ajgreeny on 2008-08-07
I run both ubuntu and mint, but prefer ubuntu, though I would find it difficult to explain exactly why.  They are both based on the same underlying code, and use basically the same repositories, with some extras for mint with its own packages there.  The one major difference between the two is that in ubuntu you have to install codecs etc etc to get full multimedia, with mint they are there from the start, out of the box.

Now I know how it takes me about 5 minutes to add all that is missing in ubuntu to get it fully running as I want.  Give both a try to see which you prefer.

### Post by SunnyRabbiera on 2008-08-08
> **molom said:**
> Install Ubuntu 8.04
Install [LSI]("http://tuxsoftware.com")
Install [USP Menu]("http://code.google.com/p/ubuntu-system-panel/")

There, you got an equivalent to Mint. Don't see whats so special about Mint if you install what I mentioned above.

Actually the differences are gigantic from my experiences.
For one the updater in Linux mint is superior to the one in Ubuntu, the One in Ubuntu installs every single update and prey to whatever deity you believe in they wont mess with the system.
Mint breaks the updates down to security updates as a primary, and all other updates pending until they are needed, there have been many times I have pulled my hair out under Ubuntu when it updated packages that would screw up my system.
Plus I find many of the bugs in ubuntu are almost always fixed in Mint, like all my monitor issues I had under Ubuntu... My monitor worked right out of the box in mint.
Then there is that terminal bug I spotted, in ubuntu I cant use the normal gnome terminal but in mint I can...

---

### Post by ezsit on 2008-08-14
> The new user wouldn't make the connection. I've been using Ubuntu for over a year now, and even now after I know a lot about Linux. I still don't make the connection that it is a easy version of Debian, because I have never used Debian, nor would I because I don't want to make things any harder for myself than they have to be.

This is irrelevant. It does not matter whether new users know the relationship between distros, if they are interested they will take the time to learn. Ubuntu is Debian with a lot of customization and some unique tools, that's about it. Ubuntu takes Debian Sid branch and creates an easier distro for people to install and use, that is all they do, technically. They do a lot more marketing and promotion than any other Linux distributor, they built and maintain a community of users better than any other distributor.

Linux Mint takes Ubuntu and adds their own customizations and unique tools, that is all they do technically. Linux Mint is also creating a community based around their creation. However, Linux Mint would be forced to rebase off of Debian proper if Ubuntu ever went the way of the dinosaur. Likewise, Ubuntu would be forced to maintain a distribution from scratch (which is a heck of a lot more work than the Ubuntu devs currently do) if the Debian community ever dried up and blew away.

My point is, all this is irrelevant to the new user. It is only after using Linux for a while and with some interest and reading, that the new user will come to appreciate the interconnectedness of Linux distributions and realize that such talk of one distro overtaking another is silly and meaningless.

---

### Post by ezsit on 2008-08-14
> They're all eroding Debian...

Not quite true. All the Debian spin-offs actually strengthen Debian and ensure Debian's importance to the world of Linux. Debian, Fedora, and Slackware are all strong distributions because they are used as the basis for many, many spin-off distributions. The more derivatives a major distro like Debian can inspire, the more essential Debian becomes to over all Linux world. Ubuntu's popularity strengthens Debian, not the other way around.

It is only the newbie that sees Ubuntu as a threat to Debian. Canonical could not even begin to create and maintain what the Debian community does, that is one of the largest Linux distributions in the world. I would not trust Canonical to do so either, since they are not governed by the Debian Social Contract. The [www.debian.org/social_contract](www.debian.org/social_contract) is what keeps Debian vital and relevant.

---

### Post by ezsit on 2008-08-16
> That's why people leave windows-constant fiddling. Geez.

With me it just just the reverse. I left the humdrum of Windows to come over to the ever-changing Linux world. I fiddle more with Linux than Windows. However, the last few Kubuntu releases have been just fantastic. Since 7.04 I have had very little to do to tweak them. First, I open a terminal and sudo passwd root to something of my choosing, then su into root (I hate sudo, hate, hate, hate!). I apt-get install:

libstdc++5
kubuntu-restricted-extras
  (includes flash, java, and other misc codecs)
vlc
mozzilla-plugin-vlc
firefox
thunderbird
kdegames
transcode
sox
lame
k9copy
soundkonverter
krita

That's about it, along with whatever dependencies these apps pull in. Sure, it took a while to learn to remeber all the names of programs I needed, and I still check the wiki page on medibuntu to copy and paste the commands to install w32codecs and libdvdcss, but all in all, it takes as long as my broadband connection takes to download the files, maybe ten minutes (i'm a slow typist). I get a fully configured, ready to go system with everything I need. Maybe this is less fiddling than with windows, its so hard to tell.

### Post by ezsit on 2008-08-16
> As the years go by, software and OSs are supposed to improve. These things are meant to get more user friendly for the end user, not stay the same. LinuxMint realizes this and make their OS more user friendly. You say that people should just learn how to use Linux, well this isn't the 1990s any more. It's been almost 20 years and Linux has come a long way. Some people just don't seem to realize this and think that thing should stay the same. Sure, let the user figure it all out. After borking the system a few time they might learn something, or they may just leave and go back to Windows.

I think you are confusing "lack of ease" with "unfamiliarity". Ubuntu is easy, drop dead easy today, especially compared with Linux from ten to fifteen years ago. No matter how easy Linux becomes, it will always be unfamiliar to those do have not used it before. 

User friendliness is a myth. The Mac was supposedly more user friendly than Windows, yet putting a Windows user in front of a Mac would make the Windows user feel dumb. Plop a Windows user down in front of Ubuntu and that user will be lost as well. It is all a matter of familiarity. 

The GUI layer in each of the three operating systems (Mac, Windows, and Linux) gives a false sense of familiarty because the GUI makes some things appear more user friendly, but all the icons do is give the user a point and click interface to the computer. A user who does not already know the difference between files, programs, directories, devices, drivers, etc., will be just as lost in a GUI as they were in a CLI. Likewise, a user who is familiar with Windows does not necessarily feel familiar with Linux or Mac beacuse the underlying systems are different. The GUI just makes the experience prettier.

At some point, the user must learn SOMETHING about what they are doing. Ubuntu is very simple to learn, and the online documentation is excellent, as are the forums. New users can ask questions and get help fairly quickly. However, a new user needs to learn something, sometime, in order to grow beyond the newbie state.

The Windows GUI got many people into using computers that really did not understand what they were doing when they started. These users learned, sometimes the hard way, how to work their computers. The same thing is happening with Linux today. Ubuntu and others have made the initial hurdle of learning Linux achievable and made it easy enough for the average user to learn. It is still necessary, as it is in the rest of one's life, to learn what you are doing.

I am not saying that software development has achieved its highest goals, there is always something more to program and something more to demand of your computer. However, the user interface is approachable and the Linux system is easy enough for newbies to get started.

---

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-08-18
> **Vadi said:**
>  Linux Mint will never surpass Ubuntu Just as Ubuntu will never ever "surpass" Debian? ;) Do you realise that this highly depends on one's personal tastes? **De gustibus non est disputandum.**

You don't like Mint ... well. Don't use it then. It's simple as that.

> **Vadi said:**
>  because it's yet another distro done by people who think "they" got it "right" Well you see: that's the funny point about open source: It's everyone's right to do *EXACTLY* that. You don't like Debian? Fine: go ahead and change it, make it the way you think is right. That's what Canonical did with Ubuntu. So why shouldn't Clement aka "clem" the author of Mint do the same? Besides, given the popularity of Mint it seems he is doing certain things right?

> **Vadi said:**
>  and it's not as if it's just Ubuntu with some things pre-installed. Big deal - latest version of gOS is ubuntu + google gadgets + green theme. Woah!  **You're wrong there.** 

Early releases of Mint e.g. Mint 2.0 "Barbara" were just that: Ubuntu + codecs preinstalled + blueish theme. Yes. But already with the following releases they more and more increased the gap between Ubuntu and Mint. Your argument is therefore based on wrong assumptions and a lack of knowledge: Mint now uses its own repositories for many things, they have their own "mint*" tools which are completely absent from Ubuntu (mintupdate, mint-ntfs, mint software portal, etc.) and therefore Mint is a distribution in its own right now; just as Ubuntu is no longer "just Debian with a brownish theme".

But hey, why bash other Linux distros?? ;) Let everyone use whatever makes them happy. There is no "right" or "wrong", it's all about one's own personal preferences. So for someone the "right" Linux distro might be Gentoo ... for someone else it might be SLES or RHEL ... or OpenSUSE ... Or Slackware .... or "Linux from Scratch" ... or their very own Linux disro ;)

Linux is about having choice and having the right to choose. ;)

---

### Post by Vadi on 2008-08-18
I don't care about distros all that much; what I do care about, and what annoys me, is the stupidly large amount of **duplicated work** going on.

Linux Mint is doing just that; unlike Ubuntu which actually contributes a lot back to Debian and at least isn't a direct fork / distro with a bunch of home-made tools & preinstalled programs, it's more or less on the "parallel".

So yes, **imho**, it is just another distro which is going it's yet another own way of "getting it right". But you're welcome to use anything you want of course, I'm not bashing anybody's personal choices here :)

### Post by Zero Prime on 2008-08-18
And what duplicated work are they doing.  Their update manager is one of the best, much better than the one included with Ubuntu.  The tools included are great.

BTW, to help debunk the myth that LM is just Ubuntu with extra themes, a few tools, and codecs, think of it like this.  After a release of the newest version of Ubuntu it takes at least a month before Mint is ready.  The Mint Devs work hard to make sure everything is the best that it can be.  They start working on the tools well before the release of the newest Ubuntu.  If it was just themes and such, then it would take about 5 minutes to make and then remaster a CD and release it as Mint.

### Post by scorp123 on 2008-08-18
> **Vadi said:**
> what I do care about, and what annoys me, is the stupidly large amount of **duplicated work** going on.  Well, "one size fits all" is exactly *NOT* what Linux is about. The freedom of open source also means that people have the freedom to reinvent the wheel and the fire again if they choose to do that, or in the case of Mint and Ubuntu: "duplicate" whatever they wish to "duplicate" or "improve" whatever their creators think that needs improvement in the distro their own creations are based on. Whether we agree with that or not is irrelevant, fact is that it is everyone's right to do so (within the rules of the GPL of course).

If I decided to start a fork of Ubuntu and thus create yet another distro ... then what? More of the "duplicated work" that seems to annoy you so much. And I have the right to do it. Who are you to tell me what I can work on or not? :D

I also assume that all those many car brands annoy you? I mean ... why so much duplicated work? Why can't everyone just drive the same one car model and be happy? Why so many different houses? Why can't we all live in the one and the same house model and be happy with it? Why so many different clothes? Why can't we all wear the same stuff? Why so many different brands of TV's, VCR's, DVD players, mobile phones .... And then all those Hollywood actors and other artists: Why do they have to sing so many different songs all the time? Why can't they all just put their efforts together, sing one particular good song, and then be done with it and not bother us anymore with new albums? Why so many movies all the time? Can't they just focus their efforts and make one good movie and then stop wasting their time making new ones? And all those artists: Why do they have to paint so many things in so many different styles??? Can't they just paint the same thing and then be happy with it? And food ... Chinese, Italian, Korean, Thai, Greek .... Why does it have to have so many different tastes?? Can't they agree on just one healthy and tasty dish and then just cook that and forget about the unnecessary rest ... ?

OMG, soooo much "duplicated work" everywhere ;)

All those "duplicated works" mean one thing: We have a choice. And life would be pretty dull without all those many possible choices ;)

### Post by DJiNN on 2008-08-20
Gotta agree with some of the above..... Linux (in all it's flavours) is great! :) As for Linux Mint, i'm using it at the moment on my main desktop (although i also have both Zenwalk & antiX on there as well). The main reason is that i haven't found another distro yet (Ubuntu included) which has pretty much EVERYTHING OOTB, and (possibly more importantly) runs faster, smoother and more responsively than both antiX and Zenwalk (Gnome version). 

I was surprised at this to be sure, as antiX has been my distro of choice for quite some time now, but it's been playing up a lot with various things. So i installed Zenwalk (Gnome) just to see what the performance was like...... Not bad, boots up fast and works well, and also looks GREAT with Gnome. But the Zenwalk repos are NOTHING compared to Debian...... So i installed the latest Mint version just to see if that was up to it. WOW!!! What a surprise...... VERY fast, and soooo smooth and responsive, with a great interface. It's faster on this machine (even with Gnome etc) than antiX is.  

Anyway, for me the point is, it's whatever works for you, the user, and that's part of what makes Linux so unique and fascinating (and also the cause of some frustration at times i guess) :)

Power to the Penguin, in all it's guises!
